neighbourhood, and take Pemberley for a kind of model. There is not a | prideandprejudice
finer county in England than Derbyshire.” | prideandprejudice
“With all my heart; I will buy Pemberley itself if Darcy will sell it.” | prideandprejudice
“I am talking of possibilities, Charles.” | prideandprejudice
“Upon my word, Caroline, I should think it more possible to get | prideandprejudice
Pemberley by purchase than by imitation.” | prideandprejudice
Elizabeth was so much caught with what passed, as to leave her very | prideandprejudice
little attention for her book; and soon laying it wholly aside, she drew | prideandprejudice
near the card-table, and stationed herself between Mr. Bingley and his | prideandprejudice
eldest sister, to observe the game. | prideandprejudice
“Is Miss Darcy much grown since the spring?” said Miss Bingley; “will | prideandprejudice
she be as tall as I am?” | prideandprejudice
“I think she will. She is now about Miss Elizabeth Bennet's height, or | prideandprejudice
rather taller.” | prideandprejudice
“How I long to see her again! I never met with anybody who delighted me | prideandprejudice
so much. Such a countenance, such manners! And so extremely accomplished | prideandprejudice
for her age! Her performance on the pianoforte is exquisite.” | prideandprejudice
﻿CHAPTER I | dracula
JONATHAN HARKER'S JOURNAL | dracula
(_Kept in shorthand._) | dracula
_3 May. Bistritz._--Left Munich at 8:35 P. M., on 1st May, arriving at | dracula
Vienna early next morning; should have arrived at 6:46, but train was an | dracula
hour late. Buda-Pesth seems a wonderful place, from the glimpse which I | dracula
got of it from the train and the little I could walk through the | dracula
streets. I feared to go very far from the station, as we had arrived | dracula
late and would start as near the correct time as possible. The | dracula
impression I had was that we were leaving the West and entering the | dracula
East; the most western of splendid bridges over the Danube, which is | dracula
here of noble width and depth, took us among the traditions of Turkish | dracula
rule. | dracula
We left in pretty good time, and came after nightfall to Klausenburgh. | dracula
Here I stopped for the night at the Hotel Royale. I had for dinner, or | dracula
rather supper, a chicken done up some way with red pepper, which was | dracula
very good but thirsty. (_Mem._, get recipe for Mina.) I asked the | dracula
waiter, and he said it was called "paprika hendl," and that, as it was a | dracula
national dish, I should be able to get it anywhere along the | dracula
Carpathians. I found my smattering of German very useful here; indeed, I | dracula
don't know how I should be able to get on without it. | dracula
Having had some time at my disposal when in London, I had visited the | dracula
British Museum, and made search among the books and maps in the library | dracula
regarding Transylvania; it had struck me that some foreknowledge of the | dracula
country could hardly fail to have some importance in dealing with a | dracula
nobleman of that country. I find that the district he named is in the | dracula
extreme east of the country, just on the borders of three states, | dracula
Transylvania, Moldavia and Bukovina, in the midst of the Carpathian | dracula
mountains; one of the wildest and least known portions of Europe. I was | dracula
not able to light on any map or work giving the exact locality of the | dracula
Castle Dracula, as there are no maps of this country as yet to compare | dracula
with our own Ordnance Survey maps; but I found that Bistritz, the post | dracula
town named by Count Dracula, is a fairly well-known place. I shall enter | dracula
here some of my notes, as they may refresh my memory when I talk over my | dracula
travels with Mina. | dracula
In the population of Transylvania there are four distinct nationalities: | dracula
Saxons in the South, and mixed with them the Wallachs, who are the | dracula
descendants of the Dacians; Magyars in the West, and Szekelys in the | dracula
East and North. I am going among the latter, who claim to be descended | dracula
from Attila and the Huns. This may be so, for when the Magyars conquered | dracula
the country in the eleventh century they found the Huns settled in it. I | dracula
read that every known superstition in the world is gathered into the | dracula
horseshoe of the Carpathians, as if it were the centre of some sort of | dracula
imaginative whirlpool; if so my stay may be very interesting. (_Mem._, I | dracula
must ask the Count all about them.) | dracula
I did not sleep well, though my bed was comfortable enough, for I had | dracula
all sorts of queer dreams. There was a dog howling all night under my | dracula
window, which may have had something to do with it; or it may have been | dracula
the paprika, for I had to drink up all the water in my carafe, and was | dracula
still thirsty. Towards morning I slept and was wakened by the continuous | dracula
knocking at my door, so I guess I must have been sleeping soundly then. | dracula
I had for breakfast more paprika, and a sort of porridge of maize flour | dracula
which they said was "mamaliga," and egg-plant stuffed with forcemeat, a | dracula
very excellent dish, which they call "impletata." (_Mem._, get recipe | dracula
for this also.) I had to hurry breakfast, for the train started a little | dracula
before eight, or rather it ought to have done so, for after rushing to | dracula
the station at 7:30 I had to sit in the carriage for more than an hour | dracula
before we began to move. It seems to me that the further east you go the | dracula
more unpunctual are the trains. What ought they to be in China? | dracula
All day long we seemed to dawdle through a country which was full of | dracula
beauty of every kind. Sometimes we saw little towns or castles on the | dracula
top of steep hills such as we see in old missals; sometimes we ran by | dracula
rivers and streams which seemed from the wide stony margin on each side | dracula
of them to be subject to great floods. It takes a lot of water, and | dracula
running strong, to sweep the outside edge of a river clear. At every | dracula
station there were groups of people, sometimes crowds, and in all sorts | dracula
of attire. Some of them were just like the peasants at home or those I | dracula
saw coming through France and Germany, with short jackets and round hats | dracula
and home-made trousers; but others were very picturesque. The women | dracula
looked pretty, except when you got near them, but they were very clumsy | dracula
about the waist. They had all full white sleeves of some kind or other, | dracula
and most of them had big belts with a lot of strips of something | dracula
fluttering from them like the dresses in a ballet, but of course there | dracula
were petticoats under them. The strangest figures we saw were the | dracula
Slovaks, who were more barbarian than the rest, with their big cow-boy | dracula
hats, great baggy dirty-white trousers, white linen shirts, and enormous | dracula
heavy leather belts, nearly a foot wide, all studded over with brass | dracula
nails. They wore high boots, with their trousers tucked into them, and | dracula
had long black hair and heavy black moustaches. They are very | dracula
picturesque, but do not look prepossessing. On the stage they would be | dracula
set down at once as some old Oriental band of brigands. They are, | dracula
however, I am told, very harmless and rather wanting in natural | dracula
self-assertion. | dracula
It was on the dark side of twilight when we got to Bistritz, which is a | dracula
very interesting old place. Being practically on the frontier--for the | dracula
Borgo Pass leads from it into Bukovina--it has had a very stormy | dracula
existence, and it certainly shows marks of it. Fifty years ago a series | dracula
of great fires took place, which made terrible havoc on five separate | dracula
occasions. At the very beginning of the seventeenth century it underwent | dracula
a siege of three weeks and lost 13,000 people, the casualties of war | dracula
proper being assisted by famine and disease. | dracula
Count Dracula had directed me to go to the Golden Krone Hotel, which I | dracula
found, to my great delight, to be thoroughly old-fashioned, for of | dracula
course I wanted to see all I could of the ways of the country. I was | dracula
evidently expected, for when I got near the door I faced a | dracula
cheery-looking elderly woman in the usual peasant dress--white | dracula
undergarment with long double apron, front, and back, of coloured stuff | dracula
fitting almost too tight for modesty. When I came close she bowed and | dracula
said, "The Herr Englishman?" "Yes," I said, "Jonathan Harker." She | dracula
smiled, and gave some message to an elderly man in white shirt-sleeves, | dracula
who had followed her to the door. He went, but immediately returned with | dracula
a letter:-- | dracula
     "My Friend.--Welcome to the Carpathians. I am anxiously expecting | dracula
     you. Sleep well to-night. At three to-morrow the diligence will | dracula
     start for Bukovina; a place on it is kept for you. At the Borgo | dracula
     Pass my carriage will await you and will bring you to me. I trust | dracula
     that your journey from London has been a happy one, and that you | dracula
     will enjoy your stay in my beautiful land. | dracula
"Your friend, | dracula
"DRACULA." | dracula
_4 May._--I found that my landlord had got a letter from the Count, | dracula
directing him to secure the best place on the coach for me; but on | dracula
making inquiries as to details he seemed somewhat reticent, and | dracula
pretended that he could not understand my German. This could not be | dracula
true, because up to then he had understood it perfectly; at least, he | dracula
answered my questions exactly as if he did. He and his wife, the old | dracula
lady who had received me, looked at each other in a frightened sort of | dracula
way. He mumbled out that the money had been sent in a letter, and that | dracula
was all he knew. When I asked him if he knew Count Dracula, and could | dracula
tell me anything of his castle, both he and his wife crossed themselves, | dracula
and, saying that they knew nothing at all, simply refused to speak | dracula
further. It was so near the time of starting that I had no time to ask | dracula
any one else, for it was all very mysterious and not by any means | dracula
comforting. | dracula
Just before I was leaving, the old lady came up to my room and said in a | dracula
very hysterical way: | dracula
"Must you go? Oh! young Herr, must you go?" She was in such an excited | dracula
state that she seemed to have lost her grip of what German she knew, and | dracula
mixed it all up with some other language which I did not know at all. I | dracula
was just able to follow her by asking many questions. When I told her | dracula
that I must go at once, and that I was engaged on important business, | dracula
she asked again: | dracula
"Do you know what day it is?" I answered that it was the fourth of May. | dracula
She shook her head as she said again: | dracula
"Oh, yes! I know that! I know that, but do you know what day it is?" On | dracula
my saying that I did not understand, she went on: | dracula
"It is the eve of St. George's Day. Do you not know that to-night, when | dracula
the clock strikes midnight, all the evil things in the world will have | dracula
full sway? Do you know where you are going, and what you are going to?" | dracula
She was in such evident distress that I tried to comfort her, but | dracula
without effect. Finally she went down on her knees and implored me not | dracula
to go; at least to wait a day or two before starting. It was all very | dracula
ridiculous but I did not feel comfortable. However, there was business | dracula
to be done, and I could allow nothing to interfere with it. I therefore | dracula
tried to raise her up, and said, as gravely as I could, that I thanked | dracula
her, but my duty was imperative, and that I must go. She then rose and | dracula
dried her eyes, and taking a crucifix from her neck offered it to me. I | dracula
did not know what to do, for, as an English Churchman, I have been | dracula
taught to regard such things as in some measure idolatrous, and yet it | dracula
seemed so ungracious to refuse an old lady meaning so well and in such a | dracula
state of mind. She saw, I suppose, the doubt in my face, for she put the | dracula
rosary round my neck, and said, "For your mother's sake," and went out | dracula
of the room. I am writing up this part of the diary whilst I am waiting | dracula
for the coach, which is, of course, late; and the crucifix is still | dracula
round my neck. Whether it is the old lady's fear, or the many ghostly | dracula
traditions of this place, or the crucifix itself, I do not know, but I | dracula
am not feeling nearly as easy in my mind as usual. If this book should | dracula
ever reach Mina before I do, let it bring my good-bye. Here comes the | dracula
coach! | dracula
       *       *       *       *       * | dracula
_5 May. The Castle._--The grey of the morning has passed, and the sun is | dracula
high over the distant horizon, which seems jagged, whether with trees or | dracula
hills I know not, for it is so far off that big things and little are | dracula
mixed. I am not sleepy, and, as I am not to be called till I awake, | dracula
naturally I write till sleep comes. There are many odd things to put | dracula
down, and, lest who reads them may fancy that I dined too well before I | dracula
left Bistritz, let me put down my dinner exactly. I dined on what they | dracula
called "robber steak"--bits of bacon, onion, and beef, seasoned with red | dracula
pepper, and strung on sticks and roasted over the fire, in the simple | dracula
style of the London cat's meat! The wine was Golden Mediasch, which | dracula
produces a queer sting on the tongue, which is, however, not | dracula
disagreeable. I had only a couple of glasses of this, and nothing else. | dracula
When I got on the coach the driver had not taken his seat, and I saw him | dracula
talking with the landlady. They were evidently talking of me, for every | dracula
now and then they looked at me, and some of the people who were sitting | dracula
on the bench outside the door--which they call by a name meaning | dracula
"word-bearer"--came and listened, and then looked at me, most of them | dracula
pityingly. I could hear a lot of words often repeated, queer words, for | dracula
there were many nationalities in the crowd; so I quietly got my polyglot | dracula
dictionary from my bag and looked them out. I must say they were not | dracula
cheering to me, for amongst them were "Ordog"--Satan, "pokol"--hell, | dracula
"stregoica"--witch, "vrolok" and "vlkoslak"--both of which mean the same | dracula
thing, one being Slovak and the other Servian for something that is | dracula
either were-wolf or vampire. (_Mem._, I must ask the Count about these | dracula
superstitions) | dracula
When we started, the crowd round the inn door, which had by this time | dracula
swelled to a considerable size, all made the sign of the cross and | dracula
pointed two fingers towards me. With some difficulty I got a | dracula
fellow-passenger to tell me what they meant; he would not answer at | dracula
first, but on learning that I was English, he explained that it was a | dracula
charm or guard against the evil eye. This was not very pleasant for me, | dracula
just starting for an unknown place to meet an unknown man; but every one | dracula
seemed so kind-hearted, and so sorrowful, and so sympathetic that I | dracula
could not but be touched. I shall never forget the last glimpse which I | dracula
had of the inn-yard and its crowd of picturesque figures, all crossing | dracula
themselves, as they stood round the wide archway, with its background of | dracula
rich foliage of oleander and orange trees in green tubs clustered in the | dracula
centre of the yard. Then our driver, whose wide linen drawers covered | dracula
the whole front of the box-seat--"gotza" they call them--cracked his big | dracula
whip over his four small horses, which ran abreast, and we set off on | dracula
our journey. | dracula
I soon lost sight and recollection of ghostly fears in the beauty of the | dracula
scene as we drove along, although had I known the language, or rather | dracula
languages, which my fellow-passengers were speaking, I might not have | dracula
been able to throw them off so easily. Before us lay a green sloping | dracula
land full of forests and woods, with here and there steep hills, crowned | dracula
with clumps of trees or with farmhouses, the blank gable end to the | dracula
road. There was everywhere a bewildering mass of fruit blossom--apple, | dracula
plum, pear, cherry; and as we drove by I could see the green grass under | dracula
the trees spangled with the fallen petals. In and out amongst these | dracula
green hills of what they call here the "Mittel Land" ran the road, | dracula
losing itself as it swept round the grassy curve, or was shut out by the | dracula
straggling ends of pine woods, which here and there ran down the | dracula
hillsides like tongues of flame. The road was rugged, but still we | dracula
seemed to fly over it with a feverish haste. I could not understand then | dracula
what the haste meant, but the driver was evidently bent on losing no | dracula
time in reaching Borgo Prund. I was told that this road is in summertime | dracula
excellent, but that it had not yet been put in order after the winter | dracula
snows. In this respect it is different from the general run of roads in | dracula
the Carpathians, for it is an old tradition that they are not to be kept | dracula
in too good order. Of old the Hospadars would not repair them, lest the | dracula
Turk should think that they were preparing to bring in foreign troops, | dracula
and so hasten the war which was always really at loading point. | dracula
Beyond the green swelling hills of the Mittel Land rose mighty slopes | dracula
of forest up to the lofty steeps of the Carpathians themselves. Right | dracula
and left of us they towered, with the afternoon sun falling full upon | dracula
them and bringing out all the glorious colours of this beautiful range, | dracula
deep blue and purple in the shadows of the peaks, green and brown where | dracula
grass and rock mingled, and an endless perspective of jagged rock and | dracula
pointed crags, till these were themselves lost in the distance, where | dracula
the snowy peaks rose grandly. Here and there seemed mighty rifts in the | dracula
mountains, through which, as the sun began to sink, we saw now and again | dracula
the white gleam of falling water. One of my companions touched my arm as | dracula
we swept round the base of a hill and opened up the lofty, snow-covered | dracula
peak of a mountain, which seemed, as we wound on our serpentine way, to | dracula
be right before us:-- | dracula
"Look! Isten szek!"--"God's seat!"--and he crossed himself reverently. | dracula
As we wound on our endless way, and the sun sank lower and lower behind | dracula
us, the shadows of the evening began to creep round us. This was | dracula
emphasised by the fact that the snowy mountain-top still held the | dracula
sunset, and seemed to glow out with a delicate cool pink. Here and there | dracula
we passed Cszeks and Slovaks, all in picturesque attire, but I noticed | dracula
that goitre was painfully prevalent. By the roadside were many crosses, | dracula
and as we swept by, my companions all crossed themselves. Here and there | dracula
was a peasant man or woman kneeling before a shrine, who did not even | dracula
turn round as we approached, but seemed in the self-surrender of | dracula
devotion to have neither eyes nor ears for the outer world. There were | dracula
many things new to me: for instance, hay-ricks in the trees, and here | dracula
and there very beautiful masses of weeping birch, their white stems | dracula
shining like silver through the delicate green of the leaves. Now and | dracula
again we passed a leiter-wagon--the ordinary peasant's cart--with its | dracula
long, snake-like vertebra, calculated to suit the inequalities of the | dracula
road. On this were sure to be seated quite a group of home-coming | dracula
peasants, the Cszeks with their white, and the Slovaks with their | dracula
coloured, sheepskins, the latter carrying lance-fashion their long | dracula
staves, with axe at end. As the evening fell it began to get very cold, | dracula
and the growing twilight seemed to merge into one dark mistiness the | dracula
gloom of the trees, oak, beech, and pine, though in the valleys which | dracula
ran deep between the spurs of the hills, as we ascended through the | dracula
Pass, the dark firs stood out here and there against the background of | dracula
late-lying snow. Sometimes, as the road was cut through the pine woods | dracula
that seemed in the darkness to be closing down upon us, great masses of | dracula
greyness, which here and there bestrewed the trees, produced a | dracula
peculiarly weird and solemn effect, which carried on the thoughts and | dracula
grim fancies engendered earlier in the evening, when the falling sunset | dracula
threw into strange relief the ghost-like clouds which amongst the | dracula
Carpathians seem to wind ceaselessly through the valleys. Sometimes the | dracula
hills were so steep that, despite our driver's haste, the horses could | dracula
only go slowly. I wished to get down and walk up them, as we do at home, | dracula
but the driver would not hear of it. "No, no," he said; "you must not | dracula
walk here; the dogs are too fierce"; and then he added, with what he | dracula
evidently meant for grim pleasantry--for he looked round to catch the | dracula
approving smile of the rest--"and you may have enough of such matters | dracula
before you go to sleep." The only stop he would make was a moment's | dracula
pause to light his lamps. | dracula
When it grew dark there seemed to be some excitement amongst the | dracula
passengers, and they kept speaking to him, one after the other, as | dracula
though urging him to further speed. He lashed the horses unmercifully | dracula
with his long whip, and with wild cries of encouragement urged them on | dracula
to further exertions. Then through the darkness I could see a sort of | dracula
patch of grey light ahead of us, as though there were a cleft in the | dracula
hills. The excitement of the passengers grew greater; the crazy coach | dracula
rocked on its great leather springs, and swayed like a boat tossed on a | dracula
stormy sea. I had to hold on. The road grew more level, and we appeared | dracula
to fly along. Then the mountains seemed to come nearer to us on each | dracula
side and to frown down upon us; we were entering on the Borgo Pass. One | dracula
by one several of the passengers offered me gifts, which they pressed | dracula
upon me with an earnestness which would take no denial; these were | dracula
certainly of an odd and varied kind, but each was given in simple good | dracula
faith, with a kindly word, and a blessing, and that strange mixture of | dracula
fear-meaning movements which I had seen outside the hotel at | dracula
Bistritz--the sign of the cross and the guard against the evil eye. | dracula
Then, as we flew along, the driver leaned forward, and on each side the | dracula
passengers, craning over the edge of the coach, peered eagerly into the | dracula
darkness. It was evident that something very exciting was either | dracula
happening or expected, but though I asked each passenger, no one would | dracula
give me the slightest explanation. This state of excitement kept on for | dracula
some little time; and at last we saw before us the Pass opening out on | dracula
the eastern side. There were dark, rolling clouds overhead, and in the | dracula
air the heavy, oppressive sense of thunder. It seemed as though the | dracula
mountain range had separated two atmospheres, and that now we had got | dracula
into the thunderous one. I was now myself looking out for the conveyance | dracula
which was to take me to the Count. Each moment I expected to see the | dracula
glare of lamps through the blackness; but all was dark. The only light | dracula
was the flickering rays of our own lamps, in which the steam from our | dracula
hard-driven horses rose in a white cloud. We could see now the sandy | dracula
road lying white before us, but there was on it no sign of a vehicle. | dracula
The passengers drew back with a sigh of gladness, which seemed to mock | dracula
my own disappointment. I was already thinking what I had best do, when | dracula
the driver, looking at his watch, said to the others something which I | dracula
could hardly hear, it was spoken so quietly and in so low a tone; I | dracula
thought it was "An hour less than the time." Then turning to me, he said | dracula
in German worse than my own:-- | dracula
"There is no carriage here. The Herr is not expected after all. He will | dracula
now come on to Bukovina, and return to-morrow or the next day; better | dracula
the next day." Whilst he was speaking the horses began to neigh and | dracula
snort and plunge wildly, so that the driver had to hold them up. Then, | dracula
amongst a chorus of screams from the peasants and a universal crossing | dracula
of themselves, a calèche, with four horses, drove up behind us, overtook | dracula
us, and drew up beside the coach. I could see from the flash of our | dracula
lamps, as the rays fell on them, that the horses were coal-black and | dracula
splendid animals. They were driven by a tall man, with a long brown | dracula
beard and a great black hat, which seemed to hide his face from us. I | dracula
could only see the gleam of a pair of very bright eyes, which seemed red | dracula
in the lamplight, as he turned to us. He said to the driver:-- | dracula
"You are early to-night, my friend." The man stammered in reply:-- | dracula
"The English Herr was in a hurry," to which the stranger replied:-- | dracula
"That is why, I suppose, you wished him to go on to Bukovina. You cannot | dracula
deceive me, my friend; I know too much, and my horses are swift." As he | dracula
spoke he smiled, and the lamplight fell on a hard-looking mouth, with | dracula
very red lips and sharp-looking teeth, as white as ivory. One of my | dracula
companions whispered to another the line from Burger's "Lenore":-- | dracula
    "Denn die Todten reiten schnell"-- | dracula
    ("For the dead travel fast.") | dracula
The strange driver evidently heard the words, for he looked up with a | dracula
gleaming smile. The passenger turned his face away, at the same time | dracula
putting out his two fingers and crossing himself. "Give me the Herr's | dracula
luggage," said the driver; and with exceeding alacrity my bags were | dracula
handed out and put in the calèche. Then I descended from the side of the | dracula
coach, as the calèche was close alongside, the driver helping me with a | dracula
hand which caught my arm in a grip of steel; his strength must have been | dracula
prodigious. Without a word he shook his reins, the horses turned, and we | dracula
swept into the darkness of the Pass. As I looked back I saw the steam | dracula
from the horses of the coach by the light of the lamps, and projected | dracula
against it the figures of my late companions crossing themselves. Then | dracula
the driver cracked his whip and called to his horses, and off they swept | dracula
on their way to Bukovina. As they sank into the darkness I felt a | dracula
strange chill, and a lonely feeling came over me; but a cloak was thrown | dracula
over my shoulders, and a rug across my knees, and the driver said in | dracula
excellent German:-- | dracula
"The night is chill, mein Herr, and my master the Count bade me take all | dracula
care of you. There is a flask of slivovitz (the plum brandy of the | dracula
country) underneath the seat, if you should require it." I did not take | dracula
any, but it was a comfort to know it was there all the same. I felt a | dracula
little strangely, and not a little frightened. I think had there been | dracula
any alternative I should have taken it, instead of prosecuting that | dracula
unknown night journey. The carriage went at a hard pace straight along, | dracula
then we made a complete turn and went along another straight road. It | dracula
seemed to me that we were simply going over and over the same ground | dracula
again; and so I took note of some salient point, and found that this was | dracula
so. I would have liked to have asked the driver what this all meant, but | dracula
I really feared to do so, for I thought that, placed as I was, any | dracula
protest would have had no effect in case there had been an intention to | dracula
delay. By-and-by, however, as I was curious to know how time was | dracula
passing, I struck a match, and by its flame looked at my watch; it was | dracula
within a few minutes of midnight. This gave me a sort of shock, for I | dracula
suppose the general superstition about midnight was increased by my | dracula
recent experiences. I waited with a sick feeling of suspense. | dracula
Then a dog began to howl somewhere in a farmhouse far down the road--a | dracula
long, agonised wailing, as if from fear. The sound was taken up by | dracula
another dog, and then another and another, till, borne on the wind which | dracula
now sighed softly through the Pass, a wild howling began, which seemed | dracula
to come from all over the country, as far as the imagination could grasp | dracula
it through the gloom of the night. At the first howl the horses began to | dracula
strain and rear, but the driver spoke to them soothingly, and they | dracula
quieted down, but shivered and sweated as though after a runaway from | dracula
sudden fright. Then, far off in the distance, from the mountains on each | dracula
side of us began a louder and a sharper howling--that of wolves--which | dracula
affected both the horses and myself in the same way--for I was minded to | dracula
jump from the calèche and run, whilst they reared again and plunged | dracula
madly, so that the driver had to use all his great strength to keep them | dracula
from bolting. In a few minutes, however, my own ears got accustomed to | dracula
the sound, and the horses so far became quiet that the driver was able | dracula
to descend and to stand before them. He petted and soothed them, and | dracula
whispered something in their ears, as I have heard of horse-tamers | dracula
doing, and with extraordinary effect, for under his caresses they became | dracula
quite manageable again, though they still trembled. The driver again | dracula
took his seat, and shaking his reins, started off at a great pace. This | dracula
time, after going to the far side of the Pass, he suddenly turned down a | dracula
narrow roadway which ran sharply to the right. | dracula
Soon we were hemmed in with trees, which in places arched right over the | dracula
roadway till we passed as through a tunnel; and again great frowning | dracula
rocks guarded us boldly on either side. Though we were in shelter, we | dracula
could hear the rising wind, for it moaned and whistled through the | dracula
rocks, and the branches of the trees crashed together as we swept along. | dracula
It grew colder and colder still, and fine, powdery snow began to fall, | dracula
so that soon we and all around us were covered with a white blanket. The | dracula
keen wind still carried the howling of the dogs, though this grew | dracula
fainter as we went on our way. The baying of the wolves sounded nearer | dracula
and nearer, as though they were closing round on us from every side. I | dracula
grew dreadfully afraid, and the horses shared my fear. The driver, | dracula
however, was not in the least disturbed; he kept turning his head to | dracula
left and right, but I could not see anything through the darkness. | dracula
Suddenly, away on our left, I saw a faint flickering blue flame. The | dracula
driver saw it at the same moment; he at once checked the horses, and, | dracula
jumping to the ground, disappeared into the darkness. I did not know | dracula
what to do, the less as the howling of the wolves grew closer; but while | dracula
I wondered the driver suddenly appeared again, and without a word took | dracula
his seat, and we resumed our journey. I think I must have fallen asleep | dracula
and kept dreaming of the incident, for it seemed to be repeated | dracula
endlessly, and now looking back, it is like a sort of awful nightmare. | dracula
Once the flame appeared so near the road, that even in the darkness | dracula
around us I could watch the driver's motions. He went rapidly to where | dracula
the blue flame arose--it must have been very faint, for it did not seem | dracula
to illumine the place around it at all--and gathering a few stones, | dracula
formed them into some device. Once there appeared a strange optical | dracula
effect: when he stood between me and the flame he did not obstruct it, | dracula
for I could see its ghostly flicker all the same. This startled me, but | dracula
as the effect was only momentary, I took it that my eyes deceived me | dracula
straining through the darkness. Then for a time there were no blue | dracula
flames, and we sped onwards through the gloom, with the howling of the | dracula
wolves around us, as though they were following in a moving circle. | dracula
At last there came a time when the driver went further afield than he | dracula
had yet gone, and during his absence, the horses began to tremble worse | dracula
than ever and to snort and scream with fright. I could not see any cause | dracula
for it, for the howling of the wolves had ceased altogether; but just | dracula
then the moon, sailing through the black clouds, appeared behind the | dracula
jagged crest of a beetling, pine-clad rock, and by its light I saw | dracula
around us a ring of wolves, with white teeth and lolling red tongues, | dracula
with long, sinewy limbs and shaggy hair. They were a hundred times more | dracula
terrible in the grim silence which held them than even when they howled. | dracula
For myself, I felt a sort of paralysis of fear. It is only when a man | dracula
feels himself face to face with such horrors that he can understand | dracula
their true import. | dracula
All at once the wolves began to howl as though the moonlight had had | dracula
some peculiar effect on them. The horses jumped about and reared, and | dracula
looked helplessly round with eyes that rolled in a way painful to see; | dracula
but the living ring of terror encompassed them on every side; and they | dracula
had perforce to remain within it. I called to the coachman to come, for | dracula
it seemed to me that our only chance was to try to break out through the | dracula
ring and to aid his approach. I shouted and beat the side of the | dracula
calèche, hoping by the noise to scare the wolves from that side, so as | dracula
to give him a chance of reaching the trap. How he came there, I know | dracula
not, but I heard his voice raised in a tone of imperious command, and | dracula
looking towards the sound, saw him stand in the roadway. As he swept his | dracula
long arms, as though brushing aside some impalpable obstacle, the wolves | dracula
fell back and back further still. Just then a heavy cloud passed across | dracula
the face of the moon, so that we were again in darkness. | dracula
When I could see again the driver was climbing into the calèche, and the | dracula
wolves had disappeared. This was all so strange and uncanny that a | dracula
dreadful fear came upon me, and I was afraid to speak or move. The time | dracula
seemed interminable as we swept on our way, now in almost complete | dracula
darkness, for the rolling clouds obscured the moon. We kept on | dracula
ascending, with occasional periods of quick descent, but in the main | dracula
always ascending. Suddenly, I became conscious of the fact that the | dracula
driver was in the act of pulling up the horses in the courtyard of a | dracula
vast ruined castle, from whose tall black windows came no ray of light, | dracula
and whose broken battlements showed a jagged line against the moonlit | dracula
sky. | dracula
CHAPTER II | dracula
JONATHAN HARKER'S JOURNAL--_continued_ | dracula
_5 May._--I must have been asleep, for certainly if I had been fully | dracula
awake I must have noticed the approach of such a remarkable place. In | dracula
the gloom the courtyard looked of considerable size, and as several dark | dracula
ways led from it under great round arches, it perhaps seemed bigger than | dracula
it really is. I have not yet been able to see it by daylight. | dracula
When the calèche stopped, the driver jumped down and held out his hand | dracula
to assist me to alight. Again I could not but notice his prodigious | dracula
strength. His hand actually seemed like a steel vice that could have | dracula
crushed mine if he had chosen. Then he took out my traps, and placed | dracula
them on the ground beside me as I stood close to a great door, old and | dracula
studded with large iron nails, and set in a projecting doorway of | dracula
massive stone. I could see even in the dim light that the stone was | dracula
massively carved, but that the carving had been much worn by time and | dracula
weather. As I stood, the driver jumped again into his seat and shook the | dracula
reins; the horses started forward, and trap and all disappeared down one | dracula
of the dark openings. | dracula
I stood in silence where I was, for I did not know what to do. Of bell | dracula
or knocker there was no sign; through these frowning walls and dark | dracula
window openings it was not likely that my voice could penetrate. The | dracula
time I waited seemed endless, and I felt doubts and fears crowding upon | dracula
me. What sort of place had I come to, and among what kind of people? | dracula
What sort of grim adventure was it on which I had embarked? Was this a | dracula
customary incident in the life of a solicitor's clerk sent out to | dracula
explain the purchase of a London estate to a foreigner? Solicitor's | dracula
clerk! Mina would not like that. Solicitor--for just before leaving | dracula
London I got word that my examination was successful; and I am now a | dracula
full-blown solicitor! I began to rub my eyes and pinch myself to see if | dracula
I were awake. It all seemed like a horrible nightmare to me, and I | dracula
expected that I should suddenly awake, and find myself at home, with | dracula
the dawn struggling in through the windows, as I had now and again felt | dracula
in the morning after a day of overwork. But my flesh answered the | dracula
pinching test, and my eyes were not to be deceived. I was indeed awake | dracula
and among the Carpathians. All I could do now was to be patient, and to | dracula
wait the coming of the morning. | dracula
Just as I had come to this conclusion I heard a heavy step approaching | dracula
behind the great door, and saw through the chinks the gleam of a coming | dracula
light. Then there was the sound of rattling chains and the clanking of | dracula
massive bolts drawn back. A key was turned with the loud grating noise | dracula
of long disuse, and the great door swung back. | dracula
Within, stood a tall old man, clean shaven save for a long white | dracula
moustache, and clad in black from head to foot, without a single speck | dracula
of colour about him anywhere. He held in his hand an antique silver | dracula
lamp, in which the flame burned without chimney or globe of any kind, | dracula
throwing long quivering shadows as it flickered in the draught of the | dracula
open door. The old man motioned me in with his right hand with a courtly | dracula
gesture, saying in excellent English, but with a strange intonation:-- | dracula
"Welcome to my house! Enter freely and of your own will!" He made no | dracula
motion of stepping to meet me, but stood like a statue, as though his | dracula
gesture of welcome had fixed him into stone. The instant, however, that | dracula
I had stepped over the threshold, he moved impulsively forward, and | dracula
holding out his hand grasped mine with a strength which made me wince, | dracula
an effect which was not lessened by the fact that it seemed as cold as | dracula
ice--more like the hand of a dead than a living man. Again he said:-- | dracula
"Welcome to my house. Come freely. Go safely; and leave something of the | dracula
happiness you bring!" The strength of the handshake was so much akin to | dracula
that which I had noticed in the driver, whose face I had not seen, that | dracula
for a moment I doubted if it were not the same person to whom I was | dracula
speaking; so to make sure, I said interrogatively:-- | dracula
"Count Dracula?" He bowed in a courtly way as he replied:-- | dracula
"I am Dracula; and I bid you welcome, Mr. Harker, to my house. Come in; | dracula
the night air is chill, and you must need to eat and rest." As he was | dracula
speaking, he put the lamp on a bracket on the wall, and stepping out, | dracula
took my luggage; he had carried it in before I could forestall him. I | dracula
protested but he insisted:-- | dracula
"Nay, sir, you are my guest. It is late, and my people are not | dracula
available. Let me see to your comfort myself." He insisted on carrying | dracula
my traps along the passage, and then up a great winding stair, and | dracula
along another great passage, on whose stone floor our steps rang | dracula
heavily. At the end of this he threw open a heavy door, and I rejoiced | dracula
to see within a well-lit room in which a table was spread for supper, | dracula
and on whose mighty hearth a great fire of logs, freshly replenished, | dracula
flamed and flared. | dracula
The Count halted, putting down my bags, closed the door, and crossing | dracula
the room, opened another door, which led into a small octagonal room lit | dracula
by a single lamp, and seemingly without a window of any sort. Passing | dracula
through this, he opened another door, and motioned me to enter. It was a | dracula
welcome sight; for here was a great bedroom well lighted and warmed with | dracula
another log fire,--also added to but lately, for the top logs were | dracula
fresh--which sent a hollow roar up the wide chimney. The Count himself | dracula
left my luggage inside and withdrew, saying, before he closed the | dracula
door:-- | dracula
"You will need, after your journey, to refresh yourself by making your | dracula
toilet. I trust you will find all you wish. When you are ready, come | dracula
into the other room, where you will find your supper prepared." | dracula
The light and warmth and the Count's courteous welcome seemed to have | dracula
dissipated all my doubts and fears. Having then reached my normal state, | dracula
I discovered that I was half famished with hunger; so making a hasty | dracula
toilet, I went into the other room. | dracula
I found supper already laid out. My host, who stood on one side of the | dracula
great fireplace, leaning against the stonework, made a graceful wave of | dracula
his hand to the table, and said:-- | dracula
"I pray you, be seated and sup how you please. You will, I trust, excuse | dracula
me that I do not join you; but I have dined already, and I do not sup." | dracula
I handed to him the sealed letter which Mr. Hawkins had entrusted to me. | dracula
He opened it and read it gravely; then, with a charming smile, he handed | dracula
it to me to read. One passage of it, at least, gave me a thrill of | dracula
pleasure. | dracula
"I must regret that an attack of gout, from which malady I am a constant | dracula
sufferer, forbids absolutely any travelling on my part for some time to | dracula
come; but I am happy to say I can send a sufficient substitute, one in | dracula
whom I have every possible confidence. He is a young man, full of energy | dracula
and talent in his own way, and of a very faithful disposition. He is | dracula
discreet and silent, and has grown into manhood in my service. He shall | dracula
be ready to attend on you when you will during his stay, and shall take | dracula
your instructions in all matters." | dracula
The Count himself came forward and took off the cover of a dish, and I | dracula
fell to at once on an excellent roast chicken. This, with some cheese | dracula
and a salad and a bottle of old Tokay, of which I had two glasses, was | dracula
my supper. During the time I was eating it the Count asked me many | dracula
questions as to my journey, and I told him by degrees all I had | dracula
experienced. | dracula
By this time I had finished my supper, and by my host's desire had drawn | dracula
up a chair by the fire and begun to smoke a cigar which he offered me, | dracula
at the same time excusing himself that he did not smoke. I had now an | dracula
opportunity of observing him, and found him of a very marked | dracula
physiognomy. | dracula
His face was a strong--a very strong--aquiline, with high bridge of the | dracula
thin nose and peculiarly arched nostrils; with lofty domed forehead, and | dracula
hair growing scantily round the temples but profusely elsewhere. His | dracula
eyebrows were very massive, almost meeting over the nose, and with bushy | dracula
hair that seemed to curl in its own profusion. The mouth, so far as I | dracula
could see it under the heavy moustache, was fixed and rather | dracula
cruel-looking, with peculiarly sharp white teeth; these protruded over | dracula
the lips, whose remarkable ruddiness showed astonishing vitality in a | dracula
man of his years. For the rest, his ears were pale, and at the tops | dracula
extremely pointed; the chin was broad and strong, and the cheeks firm | dracula
though thin. The general effect was one of extraordinary pallor. | dracula
Hitherto I had noticed the backs of his hands as they lay on his knees | dracula
in the firelight, and they had seemed rather white and fine; but seeing | dracula
them now close to me, I could not but notice that they were rather | dracula
coarse--broad, with squat fingers. Strange to say, there were hairs in | dracula
the centre of the palm. The nails were long and fine, and cut to a sharp | dracula
point. As the Count leaned over me and his hands touched me, I could not | dracula
repress a shudder. It may have been that his breath was rank, but a | dracula
horrible feeling of nausea came over me, which, do what I would, I could | dracula
not conceal. The Count, evidently noticing it, drew back; and with a | dracula
grim sort of smile, which showed more than he had yet done his | dracula
protuberant teeth, sat himself down again on his own side of the | dracula
fireplace. We were both silent for a while; and as I looked towards the | dracula
window I saw the first dim streak of the coming dawn. There seemed a | dracula
strange stillness over everything; but as I listened I heard as if from | dracula
down below in the valley the howling of many wolves. The Count's eyes | dracula
gleamed, and he said:-- | dracula
"Listen to them--the children of the night. What music they make!" | dracula
Seeing, I suppose, some expression in my face strange to him, he | dracula
added:-- | dracula
"Ah, sir, you dwellers in the city cannot enter into the feelings of the | dracula
hunter." Then he rose and said:-- | dracula
"But you must be tired. Your bedroom is all ready, and to-morrow you | dracula
shall sleep as late as you will. I have to be away till the afternoon; | dracula
so sleep well and dream well!" With a courteous bow, he opened for me | dracula
himself the door to the octagonal room, and I entered my bedroom.... | dracula
I am all in a sea of wonders. I doubt; I fear; I think strange things, | dracula
which I dare not confess to my own soul. God keep me, if only for the | dracula
sake of those dear to me! | dracula
       *       *       *       *       * | dracula
_7 May._--It is again early morning, but I have rested and enjoyed the | dracula
last twenty-four hours. I slept till late in the day, and awoke of my | dracula
own accord. When I had dressed myself I went into the room where we had | dracula
supped, and found a cold breakfast laid out, with coffee kept hot by the | dracula
pot being placed on the hearth. There was a card on the table, on which | dracula
was written:-- | dracula
"I have to be absent for a while. Do not wait for me.--D." I set to and | dracula
enjoyed a hearty meal. When I had done, I looked for a bell, so that I | dracula
might let the servants know I had finished; but I could not find one. | dracula
There are certainly odd deficiencies in the house, considering the | dracula
extraordinary evidences of wealth which are round me. The table service | dracula
is of gold, and so beautifully wrought that it must be of immense value. | dracula
The curtains and upholstery of the chairs and sofas and the hangings of | dracula
my bed are of the costliest and most beautiful fabrics, and must have | dracula
been of fabulous value when they were made, for they are centuries old, | dracula
though in excellent order. I saw something like them in Hampton Court, | dracula
but there they were worn and frayed and moth-eaten. But still in none of | dracula
the rooms is there a mirror. There is not even a toilet glass on my | dracula
table, and I had to get the little shaving glass from my bag before I | dracula
could either shave or brush my hair. I have not yet seen a servant | dracula
anywhere, or heard a sound near the castle except the howling of wolves. | dracula
Some time after I had finished my meal--I do not know whether to call it | dracula
breakfast or dinner, for it was between five and six o'clock when I had | dracula
it--I looked about for something to read, for I did not like to go about | dracula
the castle until I had asked the Count's permission. There was | dracula
absolutely nothing in the room, book, newspaper, or even writing | dracula
materials; so I opened another door in the room and found a sort of | dracula
library. The door opposite mine I tried, but found it locked. | dracula
In the library I found, to my great delight, a vast number of English | dracula
books, whole shelves full of them, and bound volumes of magazines and | dracula
newspapers. A table in the centre was littered with English magazines | dracula
and newspapers, though none of them were of very recent date. The books | dracula
were of the most varied kind--history, geography, politics, political | dracula
economy, botany, geology, law--all relating to England and English life | dracula
and customs and manners. There were even such books of reference as the | dracula
London Directory, the "Red" and "Blue" books, Whitaker's Almanac, the | dracula
Army and Navy Lists, and--it somehow gladdened my heart to see it--the | dracula
Law List. | dracula
Whilst I was looking at the books, the door opened, and the Count | dracula
entered. He saluted me in a hearty way, and hoped that I had had a good | dracula
night's rest. Then he went on:-- | dracula
"I am glad you found your way in here, for I am sure there is much that | dracula
will interest you. These companions"--and he laid his hand on some of | dracula
the books--"have been good friends to me, and for some years past, ever | dracula
since I had the idea of going to London, have given me many, many hours | dracula
of pleasure. Through them I have come to know your great England; and to | dracula
know her is to love her. I long to go through the crowded streets of | dracula
your mighty London, to be in the midst of the whirl and rush of | dracula
humanity, to share its life, its change, its death, and all that makes | dracula
it what it is. But alas! as yet I only know your tongue through books. | dracula
To you, my friend, I look that I know it to speak." | dracula
"But, Count," I said, "you know and speak English thoroughly!" He bowed | dracula
gravely. | dracula
"I thank you, my friend, for your all too-flattering estimate, but yet I | dracula
fear that I am but a little way on the road I would travel. True, I know | dracula
the grammar and the words, but yet I know not how to speak them." | dracula
"Indeed," I said, "you speak excellently." | dracula
"Not so," he answered. "Well, I know that, did I move and speak in your | dracula
London, none there are who would not know me for a stranger. That is not | dracula
enough for me. Here I am noble; I am _boyar_; the common people know me, | dracula
and I am master. But a stranger in a strange land, he is no one; men | dracula
know him not--and to know not is to care not for. I am content if I am | dracula
like the rest, so that no man stops if he see me, or pause in his | dracula
speaking if he hear my words, 'Ha, ha! a stranger!' I have been so long | dracula
master that I would be master still--or at least that none other should | dracula
be master of me. You come to me not alone as agent of my friend Peter | dracula
Hawkins, of Exeter, to tell me all about my new estate in London. You | dracula
shall, I trust, rest here with me awhile, so that by our talking I may | dracula
learn the English intonation; and I would that you tell me when I make | dracula
error, even of the smallest, in my speaking. I am sorry that I had to be | dracula
away so long to-day; but you will, I know, forgive one who has so many | dracula
important affairs in hand." | dracula
Of course I said all I could about being willing, and asked if I might | dracula
come into that room when I chose. He answered: "Yes, certainly," and | dracula
added:-- | dracula
"You may go anywhere you wish in the castle, except where the doors are | dracula
locked, where of course you will not wish to go. There is reason that | dracula
all things are as they are, and did you see with my eyes and know with | dracula
my knowledge, you would perhaps better understand." I said I was sure of | dracula
this, and then he went on:-- | dracula
"We are in Transylvania; and Transylvania is not England. Our ways are | dracula
not your ways, and there shall be to you many strange things. Nay, from | dracula
what you have told me of your experiences already, you know something of | dracula
what strange things there may be." | dracula
This led to much conversation; and as it was evident that he wanted to | dracula
talk, if only for talking's sake, I asked him many questions regarding | dracula
things that had already happened to me or come within my notice. | dracula
Sometimes he sheered off the subject, or turned the conversation by | dracula
pretending not to understand; but generally he answered all I asked most | dracula
frankly. Then as time went on, and I had got somewhat bolder, I asked | dracula
him of some of the strange things of the preceding night, as, for | dracula
instance, why the coachman went to the places where he had seen the blue | dracula
flames. He then explained to me that it was commonly believed that on a | dracula
certain night of the year--last night, in fact, when all evil spirits | dracula
are supposed to have unchecked sway--a blue flame is seen over any place | dracula
where treasure has been concealed. "That treasure has been hidden," he | dracula
went on, "in the region through which you came last night, there can be | dracula
but little doubt; for it was the ground fought over for centuries by the | dracula
Wallachian, the Saxon, and the Turk. Why, there is hardly a foot of soil | dracula
in all this region that has not been enriched by the blood of men, | dracula
patriots or invaders. In old days there were stirring times, when the | dracula
Austrian and the Hungarian came up in hordes, and the patriots went out | dracula
to meet them--men and women, the aged and the children too--and waited | dracula
their coming on the rocks above the passes, that they might sweep | dracula
destruction on them with their artificial avalanches. When the invader | dracula
was triumphant he found but little, for whatever there was had been | dracula
sheltered in the friendly soil." | dracula
"But how," said I, "can it have remained so long undiscovered, when | dracula
there is a sure index to it if men will but take the trouble to look?" | dracula
The Count smiled, and as his lips ran back over his gums, the long, | dracula
sharp, canine teeth showed out strangely; he answered:-- | dracula
"Because your peasant is at heart a coward and a fool! Those flames only | dracula
appear on one night; and on that night no man of this land will, if he | dracula
can help it, stir without his doors. And, dear sir, even if he did he | dracula
would not know what to do. Why, even the peasant that you tell me of who | dracula
marked the place of the flame would not know where to look in daylight | dracula
even for his own work. Even you would not, I dare be sworn, be able to | dracula
find these places again?" | dracula
"There you are right," I said. "I know no more than the dead where even | dracula
to look for them." Then we drifted into other matters. | dracula
"Come," he said at last, "tell me of London and of the house which you | dracula
have procured for me." With an apology for my remissness, I went into my | dracula
own room to get the papers from my bag. Whilst I was placing them in | dracula
order I heard a rattling of china and silver in the next room, and as I | dracula
passed through, noticed that the table had been cleared and the lamp | dracula
lit, for it was by this time deep into the dark. The lamps were also lit | dracula
in the study or library, and I found the Count lying on the sofa, | dracula
reading, of all things in the world, an English Bradshaw's Guide. When I | dracula
came in he cleared the books and papers from the table; and with him I | dracula
went into plans and deeds and figures of all sorts. He was interested in | dracula
everything, and asked me a myriad questions about the place and its | dracula
surroundings. He clearly had studied beforehand all he could get on the | dracula
subject of the neighbourhood, for he evidently at the end knew very much | dracula
more than I did. When I remarked this, he answered:-- | dracula
"Well, but, my friend, is it not needful that I should? When I go there | dracula
I shall be all alone, and my friend Harker Jonathan--nay, pardon me, I | dracula
fall into my country's habit of putting your patronymic first--my friend | dracula
Jonathan Harker will not be by my side to correct and aid me. He will be | dracula
in Exeter, miles away, probably working at papers of the law with my | dracula
other friend, Peter Hawkins. So!" | dracula
We went thoroughly into the business of the purchase of the estate at | dracula
Purfleet. When I had told him the facts and got his signature to the | dracula
necessary papers, and had written a letter with them ready to post to | dracula
Mr. Hawkins, he began to ask me how I had come across so suitable a | dracula
place. I read to him the notes which I had made at the time, and which I | dracula
inscribe here:-- | dracula
"At Purfleet, on a by-road, I came across just such a place as seemed to | dracula
be required, and where was displayed a dilapidated notice that the place | dracula
was for sale. It is surrounded by a high wall, of ancient structure, | dracula
built of heavy stones, and has not been repaired for a large number of | dracula
years. The closed gates are of heavy old oak and iron, all eaten with | dracula
rust. | dracula
"The estate is called Carfax, no doubt a corruption of the old _Quatre | dracula
Face_, as the house is four-sided, agreeing with the cardinal points of | dracula
the compass. It contains in all some twenty acres, quite surrounded by | dracula
the solid stone wall above mentioned. There are many trees on it, which | dracula
make it in places gloomy, and there is a deep, dark-looking pond or | dracula
small lake, evidently fed by some springs, as the water is clear and | dracula
flows away in a fair-sized stream. The house is very large and of all | dracula
periods back, I should say, to mediæval times, for one part is of stone | dracula
immensely thick, with only a few windows high up and heavily barred with | dracula
iron. It looks like part of a keep, and is close to an old chapel or | dracula
church. I could not enter it, as I had not the key of the door leading | dracula
to it from the house, but I have taken with my kodak views of it from | dracula
various points. The house has been added to, but in a very straggling | dracula
way, and I can only guess at the amount of ground it covers, which must | dracula
be very great. There are but few houses close at hand, one being a very | dracula
large house only recently added to and formed into a private lunatic | dracula
asylum. It is not, however, visible from the grounds." | dracula
When I had finished, he said:-- | dracula
"I am glad that it is old and big. I myself am of an old family, and to | dracula
live in a new house would kill me. A house cannot be made habitable in a | dracula
day; and, after all, how few days go to make up a century. I rejoice | dracula
also that there is a chapel of old times. We Transylvanian nobles love | dracula
not to think that our bones may lie amongst the common dead. I seek not | dracula
gaiety nor mirth, not the bright voluptuousness of much sunshine and | dracula
sparkling waters which please the young and gay. I am no longer young; | dracula
and my heart, through weary years of mourning over the dead, is not | dracula
attuned to mirth. Moreover, the walls of my castle are broken; the | dracula
shadows are many, and the wind breathes cold through the broken | dracula
battlements and casements. I love the shade and the shadow, and would | dracula
be alone with my thoughts when I may." Somehow his words and his look | dracula
did not seem to accord, or else it was that his cast of face made his | dracula
smile look malignant and saturnine. | dracula
Presently, with an excuse, he left me, asking me to put all my papers | dracula
together. He was some little time away, and I began to look at some of | dracula
the books around me. One was an atlas, which I found opened naturally at | dracula
England, as if that map had been much used. On looking at it I found in | dracula
certain places little rings marked, and on examining these I noticed | dracula
that one was near London on the east side, manifestly where his new | dracula
estate was situated; the other two were Exeter, and Whitby on the | dracula
Yorkshire coast. | dracula
It was the better part of an hour when the Count returned. "Aha!" he | dracula
said; "still at your books? Good! But you must not work always. Come; I | dracula
am informed that your supper is ready." He took my arm, and we went into | dracula
the next room, where I found an excellent supper ready on the table. The | dracula
Count again excused himself, as he had dined out on his being away from | dracula
home. But he sat as on the previous night, and chatted whilst I ate. | dracula
After supper I smoked, as on the last evening, and the Count stayed with | dracula
me, chatting and asking questions on every conceivable subject, hour | dracula
after hour. I felt that it was getting very late indeed, but I did not | dracula
say anything, for I felt under obligation to meet my host's wishes in | dracula
every way. I was not sleepy, as the long sleep yesterday had fortified | dracula
me; but I could not help experiencing that chill which comes over one at | dracula
the coming of the dawn, which is like, in its way, the turn of the tide. | dracula
They say that people who are near death die generally at the change to | dracula
the dawn or at the turn of the tide; any one who has when tired, and | dracula
tied as it were to his post, experienced this change in the atmosphere | dracula
can well believe it. All at once we heard the crow of a cock coming up | dracula
with preternatural shrillness through the clear morning air; Count | dracula
Dracula, jumping to his feet, said:-- | dracula
"Why, there is the morning again! How remiss I am to let you stay up so | dracula
long. You must make your conversation regarding my dear new country of | dracula
England less interesting, so that I may not forget how time flies by | dracula
us," and, with a courtly bow, he quickly left me. | dracula
I went into my own room and drew the curtains, but there was little to | dracula
notice; my window opened into the courtyard, all I could see was the | dracula
warm grey of quickening sky. So I pulled the curtains again, and have | dracula
written of this day. | dracula
       *       *       *       *       * | dracula
_8 May._--I began to fear as I wrote in this book that I was getting too | dracula
diffuse; but now I am glad that I went into detail from the first, for | dracula
there is something so strange about this place and all in it that I | dracula
cannot but feel uneasy. I wish I were safe out of it, or that I had | dracula
never come. It may be that this strange night-existence is telling on | dracula
me; but would that that were all! If there were any one to talk to I | dracula
could bear it, but there is no one. I have only the Count to speak with, | dracula
and he!--I fear I am myself the only living soul within the place. Let | dracula
me be prosaic so far as facts can be; it will help me to bear up, and | dracula
imagination must not run riot with me. If it does I am lost. Let me say | dracula
at once how I stand--or seem to. | dracula
I only slept a few hours when I went to bed, and feeling that I could | dracula
not sleep any more, got up. I had hung my shaving glass by the window, | dracula
and was just beginning to shave. Suddenly I felt a hand on my shoulder, | dracula
and heard the Count's voice saying to me, "Good-morning." I started, for | dracula
it amazed me that I had not seen him, since the reflection of the glass | dracula
covered the whole room behind me. In starting I had cut myself slightly, | dracula
but did not notice it at the moment. Having answered the Count's | dracula
salutation, I turned to the glass again to see how I had been mistaken. | dracula
This time there could be no error, for the man was close to me, and I | dracula
could see him over my shoulder. But there was no reflection of him in | dracula
the mirror! The whole room behind me was displayed; but there was no | dracula
sign of a man in it, except myself. This was startling, and, coming on | dracula
the top of so many strange things, was beginning to increase that vague | dracula
feeling of uneasiness which I always have when the Count is near; but at | dracula
the instant I saw that the cut had bled a little, and the blood was | dracula
trickling over my chin. I laid down the razor, turning as I did so half | dracula
round to look for some sticking plaster. When the Count saw my face, his | dracula
eyes blazed with a sort of demoniac fury, and he suddenly made a grab at | dracula
my throat. I drew away, and his hand touched the string of beads which | dracula
held the crucifix. It made an instant change in him, for the fury passed | dracula
so quickly that I could hardly believe that it was ever there. | dracula
"Take care," he said, "take care how you cut yourself. It is more | dracula
dangerous than you think in this country." Then seizing the shaving | dracula
glass, he went on: "And this is the wretched thing that has done the | dracula
mischief. It is a foul bauble of man's vanity. Away with it!" and | dracula
opening the heavy window with one wrench of his terrible hand, he flung | dracula
out the glass, which was shattered into a thousand pieces on the stones | dracula
of the courtyard far below. Then he withdrew without a word. It is very | dracula
annoying, for I do not see how I am to shave, unless in my watch-case or | dracula
the bottom of the shaving-pot, which is fortunately of metal. | dracula
When I went into the dining-room, breakfast was prepared; but I could | dracula
not find the Count anywhere. So I breakfasted alone. It is strange that | dracula
as yet I have not seen the Count eat or drink. He must be a very | dracula
peculiar man! After breakfast I did a little exploring in the castle. I | dracula
went out on the stairs, and found a room looking towards the South. The | dracula
view was magnificent, and from where I stood there was every opportunity | dracula
of seeing it. The castle is on the very edge of a terrible precipice. A | dracula
stone falling from the window would fall a thousand feet without | dracula
touching anything! As far as the eye can reach is a sea of green tree | dracula
tops, with occasionally a deep rift where there is a chasm. Here and | dracula
there are silver threads where the rivers wind in deep gorges through | dracula
the forests. | dracula
But I am not in heart to describe beauty, for when I had seen the view I | dracula
explored further; doors, doors, doors everywhere, and all locked and | dracula
bolted. In no place save from the windows in the castle walls is there | dracula
an available exit. | dracula
The castle is a veritable prison, and I am a prisoner! | dracula
CHAPTER III | dracula
JONATHAN HARKER'S JOURNAL--_continued_ | dracula
When I found that I was a prisoner a sort of wild feeling came over me. | dracula
I rushed up and down the stairs, trying every door and peering out of | dracula
every window I could find; but after a little the conviction of my | dracula
helplessness overpowered all other feelings. When I look back after a | dracula
few hours I think I must have been mad for the time, for I behaved much | dracula
as a rat does in a trap. When, however, the conviction had come to me | dracula
that I was helpless I sat down quietly--as quietly as I have ever done | dracula
anything in my life--and began to think over what was best to be done. I | dracula
am thinking still, and as yet have come to no definite conclusion. Of | dracula
one thing only am I certain; that it is no use making my ideas known to | dracula
the Count. He knows well that I am imprisoned; and as he has done it | dracula
himself, and has doubtless his own motives for it, he would only deceive | dracula
me if I trusted him fully with the facts. So far as I can see, my only | dracula
plan will be to keep my knowledge and my fears to myself, and my eyes | dracula
open. I am, I know, either being deceived, like a baby, by my own fears, | dracula
or else I am in desperate straits; and if the latter be so, I need, and | dracula
shall need, all my brains to get through. | dracula
I had hardly come to this conclusion when I heard the great door below | dracula
shut, and knew that the Count had returned. He did not come at once into | dracula
the library, so I went cautiously to my own room and found him making | dracula
the bed. This was odd, but only confirmed what I had all along | dracula
thought--that there were no servants in the house. When later I saw him | dracula
through the chink of the hinges of the door laying the table in the | dracula
dining-room, I was assured of it; for if he does himself all these | dracula
menial offices, surely it is proof that there is no one else to do them. | dracula
This gave me a fright, for if there is no one else in the castle, it | dracula
must have been the Count himself who was the driver of the coach that | dracula
brought me here. This is a terrible thought; for if so, what does it | dracula
mean that he could control the wolves, as he did, by only holding up his | dracula
hand in silence. How was it that all the people at Bistritz and on the | dracula
coach had some terrible fear for me? What meant the giving of the | dracula
crucifix, of the garlic, of the wild rose, of the mountain ash? Bless | dracula
that good, good woman who hung the crucifix round my neck! for it is a | dracula
comfort and a strength to me whenever I touch it. It is odd that a thing | dracula
which I have been taught to regard with disfavour and as idolatrous | dracula
should in a time of loneliness and trouble be of help. Is it that there | dracula
is something in the essence of the thing itself, or that it is a medium, | dracula
a tangible help, in conveying memories of sympathy and comfort? Some | dracula
time, if it may be, I must examine this matter and try to make up my | dracula
mind about it. In the meantime I must find out all I can about Count | dracula
Dracula, as it may help me to understand. To-night he may talk of | dracula
himself, if I turn the conversation that way. I must be very careful, | dracula
however, not to awake his suspicion. | dracula
       *       *       *       *       * | dracula
_Midnight._--I have had a long talk with the Count. I asked him a few | dracula
questions on Transylvania history, and he warmed up to the subject | dracula
wonderfully. In his speaking of things and people, and especially of | dracula
battles, he spoke as if he had been present at them all. This he | dracula
afterwards explained by saying that to a _boyar_ the pride of his house | dracula
and name is his own pride, that their glory is his glory, that their | dracula
fate is his fate. Whenever he spoke of his house he always said "we," | dracula
and spoke almost in the plural, like a king speaking. I wish I could put | dracula
down all he said exactly as he said it, for to me it was most | dracula
fascinating. It seemed to have in it a whole history of the country. He | dracula
grew excited as he spoke, and walked about the room pulling his great | dracula
white moustache and grasping anything on which he laid his hands as | dracula
though he would crush it by main strength. One thing he said which I | dracula
shall put down as nearly as I can; for it tells in its way the story of | dracula
his race:-- | dracula
"We Szekelys have a right to be proud, for in our veins flows the blood | dracula
of many brave races who fought as the lion fights, for lordship. Here, | dracula
in the whirlpool of European races, the Ugric tribe bore down from | dracula
Iceland the fighting spirit which Thor and Wodin gave them, which their | dracula
Berserkers displayed to such fell intent on the seaboards of Europe, ay, | dracula
and of Asia and Africa too, till the peoples thought that the | dracula
were-wolves themselves had come. Here, too, when they came, they found | dracula
the Huns, whose warlike fury had swept the earth like a living flame, | dracula
till the dying peoples held that in their veins ran the blood of those | dracula
old witches, who, expelled from Scythia had mated with the devils in the | dracula
desert. Fools, fools! What devil or what witch was ever so great as | dracula
Attila, whose blood is in these veins?" He held up his arms. "Is it a | dracula
wonder that we were a conquering race; that we were proud; that when the | dracula
Magyar, the Lombard, the Avar, the Bulgar, or the Turk poured his | dracula
thousands on our frontiers, we drove them back? Is it strange that when | dracula
Arpad and his legions swept through the Hungarian fatherland he found us | dracula
here when he reached the frontier; that the Honfoglalas was completed | dracula
there? And when the Hungarian flood swept eastward, the Szekelys were | dracula
claimed as kindred by the victorious Magyars, and to us for centuries | dracula
was trusted the guarding of the frontier of Turkey-land; ay, and more | dracula
than that, endless duty of the frontier guard, for, as the Turks say, | dracula
'water sleeps, and enemy is sleepless.' Who more gladly than we | dracula
throughout the Four Nations received the 'bloody sword,' or at its | dracula
warlike call flocked quicker to the standard of the King? When was | dracula
redeemed that great shame of my nation, the shame of Cassova, when the | dracula
flags of the Wallach and the Magyar went down beneath the Crescent? Who | dracula
was it but one of my own race who as Voivode crossed the Danube and beat | dracula
the Turk on his own ground? This was a Dracula indeed! Woe was it that | dracula
his own unworthy brother, when he had fallen, sold his people to the | dracula
Turk and brought the shame of slavery on them! Was it not this Dracula, | dracula
indeed, who inspired that other of his race who in a later age again and | dracula
again brought his forces over the great river into Turkey-land; who, | dracula
when he was beaten back, came again, and again, and again, though he had | dracula
to come alone from the bloody field where his troops were being | dracula
slaughtered, since he knew that he alone could ultimately triumph! They | dracula
said that he thought only of himself. Bah! what good are peasants | dracula
without a leader? Where ends the war without a brain and heart to | dracula
conduct it? Again, when, after the battle of Mohács, we threw off the | dracula
Hungarian yoke, we of the Dracula blood were amongst their leaders, for | dracula
our spirit would not brook that we were not free. Ah, young sir, the | dracula
Szekelys--and the Dracula as their heart's blood, their brains, and | dracula
their swords--can boast a record that mushroom growths like the | dracula
Hapsburgs and the Romanoffs can never reach. The warlike days are over. | dracula
Blood is too precious a thing in these days of dishonourable peace; and | dracula
