The Old Sea-dog at the Admiral Benbow | treasureisland
SQUIRE TRELAWNEY, Dr. Livesey, and the rest of these gentlemen having | treasureisland
asked me to write down the whole particulars about Treasure Island, from | treasureisland
the beginning to the end, keeping nothing back but the bearings of the | treasureisland
island, and that only because there is still treasure not yet lifted, I | treasureisland
take up my pen in the year of grace 17__ and go back to the time when | treasureisland
my father kept the Admiral Benbow inn and the brown old seaman with the | treasureisland
sabre cut first took up his lodging under our roof. | treasureisland
I remember him as if it were yesterday, as he came plodding to the | treasureisland
inn door, his sea-chest following behind him in a hand-barrow--a | treasureisland
tall, strong, heavy, nut-brown man, his tarry pigtail falling over the | treasureisland
shoulder of his soiled blue coat, his hands ragged and scarred, with | treasureisland
black, broken nails, and the sabre cut across one cheek, a dirty, livid | treasureisland
white. I remember him looking round the cover and whistling to himself | treasureisland
as he did so, and then breaking out in that old sea-song that he sang so | treasureisland
often afterwards: | treasureisland
          “Fifteen men on the dead man's chest-- | treasureisland
             Yo-ho-ho, and a bottle of rum!” | treasureisland
in the high, old tottering voice that seemed to have been tuned and | treasureisland
broken at the capstan bars. Then he rapped on the door with a bit of | treasureisland
stick like a handspike that he carried, and when my father appeared, | treasureisland
called roughly for a glass of rum. This, when it was brought to him, | treasureisland
he drank slowly, like a connoisseur, lingering on the taste and still | treasureisland
looking about him at the cliffs and up at our signboard. | treasureisland
“This is a handy cove,” says he at length; “and a pleasant sittyated | treasureisland
grog-shop. Much company, mate?” | treasureisland
My father told him no, very little company, the more was the pity. | treasureisland
“Well, then,” said he, “this is the berth for me. Here you, matey,” he | treasureisland
cried to the man who trundled the barrow; “bring up alongside and help | treasureisland
up my chest. I'll stay here a bit,” he continued. “I'm a plain man; rum | treasureisland
and bacon and eggs is what I want, and that head up there for to watch | treasureisland
ships off. What you mought call me? You mought call me captain. Oh, I | treasureisland
see what you're at--there”; and he threw down three or four gold pieces | treasureisland
on the threshold. “You can tell me when I've worked through that,” says | treasureisland
he, looking as fierce as a commander. | treasureisland
And indeed bad as his clothes were and coarsely as he spoke, he had none | treasureisland
of the appearance of a man who sailed before the mast, but seemed like | treasureisland
a mate or skipper accustomed to be obeyed or to strike. The man who came | treasureisland
with the barrow told us the mail had set him down the morning before at | treasureisland
the Royal George, that he had inquired what inns there were along the | treasureisland
coast, and hearing ours well spoken of, I suppose, and described as | treasureisland
lonely, had chosen it from the others for his place of residence. And | treasureisland
that was all we could learn of our guest. | treasureisland
He was a very silent man by custom. All day he hung round the cove or | treasureisland
upon the cliffs with a brass telescope; all evening he sat in a corner | treasureisland
of the parlour next the fire and drank rum and water very strong. Mostly | treasureisland
he would not speak when spoken to, only look up sudden and fierce and | treasureisland
blow through his nose like a fog-horn; and we and the people who came | treasureisland
about our house soon learned to let him be. Every day when he came back | treasureisland
from his stroll he would ask if any seafaring men had gone by along the | treasureisland
road. At first we thought it was the want of company of his own kind | treasureisland
that made him ask this question, but at last we began to see he was | treasureisland
desirous to avoid them. When a seaman did put up at the Admiral Benbow | treasureisland
(as now and then some did, making by the coast road for Bristol) he | treasureisland
would look in at him through the curtained door before he entered the | treasureisland
parlour; and he was always sure to be as silent as a mouse when any such | treasureisland
was present. For me, at least, there was no secret about the matter, for | treasureisland
I was, in a way, a sharer in his alarms. He had taken me aside one day | treasureisland
and promised me a silver fourpenny on the first of every month if I | treasureisland
would only keep my “weather-eye open for a seafaring man with one leg” | treasureisland
 and let him know the moment he appeared. Often enough when the first | treasureisland
of the month came round and I applied to him for my wage, he would only | treasureisland
blow through his nose at me and stare me down, but before the week was | treasureisland
out he was sure to think better of it, bring me my four-penny piece, and | treasureisland
repeat his orders to look out for “the seafaring man with one leg.” | treasureisland
How that personage haunted my dreams, I need scarcely tell you. On | treasureisland
stormy nights, when the wind shook the four corners of the house and | treasureisland
the surf roared along the cove and up the cliffs, I would see him in a | treasureisland
thousand forms, and with a thousand diabolical expressions. Now the leg | treasureisland
would be cut off at the knee, now at the hip; now he was a monstrous | treasureisland
kind of a creature who had never had but the one leg, and that in the | treasureisland
middle of his body. To see him leap and run and pursue me over hedge and | treasureisland
ditch was the worst of nightmares. And altogether I paid pretty dear for | treasureisland
my monthly fourpenny piece, in the shape of these abominable fancies. | treasureisland
But though I was so terrified by the idea of the seafaring man with one | treasureisland
leg, I was far less afraid of the captain himself than anybody else who | treasureisland
knew him. There were nights when he took a deal more rum and water | treasureisland
than his head would carry; and then he would sometimes sit and sing his | treasureisland
wicked, old, wild sea-songs, minding nobody; but sometimes he would call | treasureisland
for glasses round and force all the trembling company to listen to his | treasureisland
stories or bear a chorus to his singing. Often I have heard the house | treasureisland
shaking with “Yo-ho-ho, and a bottle of rum,” all the neighbours joining | treasureisland
in for dear life, with the fear of death upon them, and each singing | treasureisland
louder than the other to avoid remark. For in these fits he was the most | treasureisland
overriding companion ever known; he would slap his hand on the table for | treasureisland
silence all round; he would fly up in a passion of anger at a question, | treasureisland
or sometimes because none was put, and so he judged the company was not | treasureisland
following his story. Nor would he allow anyone to leave the inn till he | treasureisland
had drunk himself sleepy and reeled off to bed. | treasureisland
His stories were what frightened people worst of all. Dreadful stories | treasureisland
they were--about hanging, and walking the plank, and storms at sea, and | treasureisland
the Dry Tortugas, and wild deeds and places on the Spanish Main. By his | treasureisland
own account he must have lived his life among some of the wickedest men | treasureisland
that God ever allowed upon the sea, and the language in which he told | treasureisland
these stories shocked our plain country people almost as much as the | treasureisland
crimes that he described. My father was always saying the inn would be | treasureisland
ruined, for people would soon cease coming there to be tyrannized over | treasureisland
and put down, and sent shivering to their beds; but I really believe his | treasureisland
presence did us good. People were frightened at the time, but on looking | treasureisland
back they rather liked it; it was a fine excitement in a quiet country | treasureisland
life, and there was even a party of the younger men who pretended to | treasureisland
admire him, calling him a “true sea-dog” and a “real old salt” and | treasureisland
such like names, and saying there was the sort of man that made England | treasureisland
terrible at sea. | treasureisland
In one way, indeed, he bade fair to ruin us, for he kept on staying week | treasureisland
after week, and at last month after month, so that all the money had | treasureisland
been long exhausted, and still my father never plucked up the heart to | treasureisland
insist on having more. If ever he mentioned it, the captain blew through | treasureisland
his nose so loudly that you might say he roared, and stared my poor | treasureisland
father out of the room. I have seen him wringing his hands after such a | treasureisland
rebuff, and I am sure the annoyance and the terror he lived in must have | treasureisland
greatly hastened his early and unhappy death. | treasureisland
All the time he lived with us the captain made no change whatever in his | treasureisland
dress but to buy some stockings from a hawker. One of the cocks of his | treasureisland
hat having fallen down, he let it hang from that day forth, though it | treasureisland
was a great annoyance when it blew. I remember the appearance of his | treasureisland
coat, which he patched himself upstairs in his room, and which, before | treasureisland
the end, was nothing but patches. He never wrote or received a letter, | treasureisland
and he never spoke with any but the neighbours, and with these, for the | treasureisland
most part, only when drunk on rum. The great sea-chest none of us had | treasureisland
ever seen open. | treasureisland
He was only once crossed, and that was towards the end, when my poor | treasureisland
father was far gone in a decline that took him off. Dr. Livesey came | treasureisland
late one afternoon to see the patient, took a bit of dinner from my | treasureisland
mother, and went into the parlour to smoke a pipe until his horse should | treasureisland
come down from the hamlet, for we had no stabling at the old Benbow. I | treasureisland
followed him in, and I remember observing the contrast the neat, bright | treasureisland
doctor, with his powder as white as snow and his bright, black eyes and | treasureisland
pleasant manners, made with the coltish country folk, and above all, | treasureisland
with that filthy, heavy, bleared scarecrow of a pirate of ours, sitting, | treasureisland
far gone in rum, with his arms on the table. Suddenly he--the captain, | treasureisland
that is--began to pipe up his eternal song: | treasureisland
          “Fifteen men on the dead man's chest-- | treasureisland
             Yo-ho-ho, and a bottle of rum! | treasureisland
           Drink and the devil had done for the rest-- | treasureisland
             Yo-ho-ho, and a bottle of rum!” | treasureisland
At first I had supposed “the dead man's chest” to be that identical big | treasureisland
box of his upstairs in the front room, and the thought had been mingled | treasureisland
in my nightmares with that of the one-legged seafaring man. But by this | treasureisland
time we had all long ceased to pay any particular notice to the song; it | treasureisland
was new, that night, to nobody but Dr. Livesey, and on him I observed it | treasureisland
did not produce an agreeable effect, for he looked up for a moment quite | treasureisland
angrily before he went on with his talk to old Taylor, the gardener, on | treasureisland
a new cure for the rheumatics. In the meantime, the captain gradually | treasureisland
brightened up at his own music, and at last flapped his hand upon | treasureisland
the table before him in a way we all knew to mean silence. The voices | treasureisland
stopped at once, all but Dr. Livesey's; he went on as before speaking | treasureisland
clear and kind and drawing briskly at his pipe between every word or | treasureisland
two. The captain glared at him for a while, flapped his hand again, | treasureisland
glared still harder, and at last broke out with a villainous, low oath, | treasureisland
“Silence, there, between decks!” | treasureisland
“Were you addressing me, sir?” says the doctor; and when the ruffian had | treasureisland
told him, with another oath, that this was so, “I have only one thing to | treasureisland
say to you, sir,” replies the doctor, “that if you keep on drinking rum, | treasureisland
the world will soon be quit of a very dirty scoundrel!” | treasureisland
The old fellow's fury was awful. He sprang to his feet, drew and opened | treasureisland
a sailor's clasp-knife, and balancing it open on the palm of his hand, | treasureisland
threatened to pin the doctor to the wall. | treasureisland
The doctor never so much as moved. He spoke to him as before, over his | treasureisland
shoulder and in the same tone of voice, rather high, so that all the | treasureisland
room might hear, but perfectly calm and steady: “If you do not put that | treasureisland
knife this instant in your pocket, I promise, upon my honour, you shall | treasureisland
hang at the next assizes.” | treasureisland
Then followed a battle of looks between them, but the captain soon | treasureisland
knuckled under, put up his weapon, and resumed his seat, grumbling like | treasureisland
a beaten dog. | treasureisland
“And now, sir,” continued the doctor, “since I now know there's such a | treasureisland
fellow in my district, you may count I'll have an eye upon you day and | treasureisland
night. I'm not a doctor only; I'm a magistrate; and if I catch a breath | treasureisland
of complaint against you, if it's only for a piece of incivility like | treasureisland
tonight's, I'll take effectual means to have you hunted down and routed | treasureisland
out of this. Let that suffice.” | treasureisland
Soon after, Dr. Livesey's horse came to the door and he rode away, but | treasureisland
the captain held his peace that evening, and for many evenings to come. | treasureisland
2 | treasureisland
Black Dog Appears and Disappears | treasureisland
IT was not very long after this that there occurred the first of the | treasureisland
mysterious events that rid us at last of the captain, though not, as you | treasureisland
will see, of his affairs. It was a bitter cold winter, with long, hard | treasureisland
frosts and heavy gales; and it was plain from the first that my poor | treasureisland
father was little likely to see the spring. He sank daily, and my mother | treasureisland
and I had all the inn upon our hands, and were kept busy enough without | treasureisland
paying much regard to our unpleasant guest. | treasureisland
It was one January morning, very early--a pinching, frosty morning--the | treasureisland
cove all grey with hoar-frost, the ripple lapping softly on the stones, | treasureisland
the sun still low and only touching the hilltops and shining far to | treasureisland
seaward. The captain had risen earlier than usual and set out down the | treasureisland
beach, his cutlass swinging under the broad skirts of the old blue coat, | treasureisland
his brass telescope under his arm, his hat tilted back upon his head. I | treasureisland
remember his breath hanging like smoke in his wake as he strode off, and | treasureisland
the last sound I heard of him as he turned the big rock was a loud snort | treasureisland
of indignation, as though his mind was still running upon Dr. Livesey. | treasureisland
Well, mother was upstairs with father and I was laying the | treasureisland
breakfast-table against the captain's return when the parlour door | treasureisland
opened and a man stepped in on whom I had never set my eyes before. He | treasureisland
was a pale, tallowy creature, wanting two fingers of the left hand, and | treasureisland
though he wore a cutlass, he did not look much like a fighter. I | treasureisland
had always my eye open for seafaring men, with one leg or two, and I | treasureisland
remember this one puzzled me. He was not sailorly, and yet he had a | treasureisland
smack of the sea about him too. | treasureisland
I asked him what was for his service, and he said he would take rum; but | treasureisland
as I was going out of the room to fetch it, he sat down upon a table | treasureisland
and motioned me to draw near. I paused where I was, with my napkin in my | treasureisland
hand. | treasureisland
“Come here, sonny,” says he. “Come nearer here.” | treasureisland
I took a step nearer. | treasureisland
“Is this here table for my mate Bill?” he asked with a kind of leer. | treasureisland
I told him I did not know his mate Bill, and this was for a person who | treasureisland
stayed in our house whom we called the captain. | treasureisland
“Well,” said he, “my mate Bill would be called the captain, as like | treasureisland
as not. He has a cut on one cheek and a mighty pleasant way with him, | treasureisland
particularly in drink, has my mate Bill. We'll put it, for argument | treasureisland
like, that your captain has a cut on one cheek--and we'll put it, if you | treasureisland
like, that that cheek's the right one. Ah, well! I told you. Now, is my | treasureisland
mate Bill in this here house?” | treasureisland
I told him he was out walking. | treasureisland
“Which way, sonny? Which way is he gone?” | treasureisland
And when I had pointed out the rock and told him how the captain was | treasureisland
likely to return, and how soon, and answered a few other questions, | treasureisland
“Ah,” said he, “this'll be as good as drink to my mate Bill.” | treasureisland
The expression of his face as he said these words was not at all | treasureisland
pleasant, and I had my own reasons for thinking that the stranger was | treasureisland
mistaken, even supposing he meant what he said. But it was no affair of | treasureisland
mine, I thought; and besides, it was difficult to know what to do. The | treasureisland
stranger kept hanging about just inside the inn door, peering round the | treasureisland
corner like a cat waiting for a mouse. Once I stepped out myself into | treasureisland
the road, but he immediately called me back, and as I did not obey quick | treasureisland
enough for his fancy, a most horrible change came over his tallowy face, | treasureisland
and he ordered me in with an oath that made me jump. As soon as I | treasureisland
was back again he returned to his former manner, half fawning, half | treasureisland
sneering, patted me on the shoulder, told me I was a good boy and he had | treasureisland
taken quite a fancy to me. “I have a son of my own,” said he, “as like | treasureisland
you as two blocks, and he's all the pride of my 'art. But the great | treasureisland
thing for boys is discipline, sonny--discipline. Now, if you had sailed | treasureisland
along of Bill, you wouldn't have stood there to be spoke to twice--not | treasureisland
you. That was never Bill's way, nor the way of sich as sailed with him. | treasureisland
And here, sure enough, is my mate Bill, with a spy-glass under his arm, | treasureisland
bless his old 'art, to be sure. You and me'll just go back into the | treasureisland
parlour, sonny, and get behind the door, and we'll give Bill a little | treasureisland
surprise--bless his 'art, I say again.” | treasureisland
So saying, the stranger backed along with me into the parlour and put me | treasureisland
behind him in the corner so that we were both hidden by the open door. I | treasureisland
was very uneasy and alarmed, as you may fancy, and it rather added to my | treasureisland
fears to observe that the stranger was certainly frightened himself. He | treasureisland
cleared the hilt of his cutlass and loosened the blade in the sheath; | treasureisland
and all the time we were waiting there he kept swallowing as if he felt | treasureisland
what we used to call a lump in the throat. | treasureisland
At last in strode the captain, slammed the door behind him, without | treasureisland
looking to the right or left, and marched straight across the room to | treasureisland
where his breakfast awaited him. | treasureisland
“Bill,” said the stranger in a voice that I thought he had tried to make | treasureisland
bold and big. | treasureisland
The captain spun round on his heel and fronted us; all the brown had | treasureisland
gone out of his face, and even his nose was blue; he had the look of a | treasureisland
man who sees a ghost, or the evil one, or something worse, if anything | treasureisland
can be; and upon my word, I felt sorry to see him all in a moment turn | treasureisland
so old and sick. | treasureisland
“Come, Bill, you know me; you know an old shipmate, Bill, surely,” said | treasureisland
the stranger. | treasureisland
The captain made a sort of gasp. | treasureisland
“Black Dog!” said he. | treasureisland
“And who else?” returned the other, getting more at his ease. “Black | treasureisland
Dog as ever was, come for to see his old shipmate Billy, at the Admiral | treasureisland
Benbow inn. Ah, Bill, Bill, we have seen a sight of times, us two, since | treasureisland
I lost them two talons,” holding up his mutilated hand. | treasureisland
“Now, look here,” said the captain; “you've run me down; here I am; | treasureisland
well, then, speak up; what is it?” | treasureisland
“That's you, Bill,” returned Black Dog, “you're in the right of it, | treasureisland
Billy. I'll have a glass of rum from this dear child here, as I've took | treasureisland
such a liking to; and we'll sit down, if you please, and talk square, | treasureisland
like old shipmates.” | treasureisland
When I returned with the rum, they were already seated on either side | treasureisland
of the captain's breakfast-table--Black Dog next to the door and | treasureisland
sitting sideways so as to have one eye on his old shipmate and one, as I | treasureisland
thought, on his retreat. | treasureisland
He bade me go and leave the door wide open. “None of your keyholes for | treasureisland
me, sonny,” he said; and I left them together and retired into the bar. | treasureisland
For a long time, though I certainly did my best to listen, I could hear | treasureisland
nothing but a low gattling; but at last the voices began to grow higher, | treasureisland
and I could pick up a word or two, mostly oaths, from the captain. | treasureisland
“No, no, no, no; and an end of it!” he cried once. And again, “If it | treasureisland
comes to swinging, swing all, say I.” | treasureisland
Then all of a sudden there was a tremendous explosion of oaths and | treasureisland
other noises--the chair and table went over in a lump, a clash of steel | treasureisland
followed, and then a cry of pain, and the next instant I saw Black | treasureisland
Dog in full flight, and the captain hotly pursuing, both with drawn | treasureisland
cutlasses, and the former streaming blood from the left shoulder. Just | treasureisland
at the door the captain aimed at the fugitive one last tremendous | treasureisland
cut, which would certainly have split him to the chine had it not been | treasureisland
intercepted by our big signboard of Admiral Benbow. You may see the | treasureisland
notch on the lower side of the frame to this day. | treasureisland
That blow was the last of the battle. Once out upon the road, Black | treasureisland
Dog, in spite of his wound, showed a wonderful clean pair of heels and | treasureisland
disappeared over the edge of the hill in half a minute. The captain, for | treasureisland
his part, stood staring at the signboard like a bewildered man. Then he | treasureisland
passed his hand over his eyes several times and at last turned back into | treasureisland
the house. | treasureisland
“Jim,” says he, “rum”; and as he spoke, he reeled a little, and caught | treasureisland
himself with one hand against the wall. | treasureisland
“Are you hurt?” cried I. | treasureisland
“Rum,” he repeated. “I must get away from here. Rum! Rum!” | treasureisland
I ran to fetch it, but I was quite unsteadied by all that had fallen | treasureisland
out, and I broke one glass and fouled the tap, and while I was still | treasureisland
getting in my own way, I heard a loud fall in the parlour, and running | treasureisland
in, beheld the captain lying full length upon the floor. At the same | treasureisland
instant my mother, alarmed by the cries and fighting, came running | treasureisland
downstairs to help me. Between us we raised his head. He was breathing | treasureisland
very loud and hard, but his eyes were closed and his face a horrible | treasureisland
colour. | treasureisland
“Dear, deary me,” cried my mother, “what a disgrace upon the house! And | treasureisland
your poor father sick!” | treasureisland
In the meantime, we had no idea what to do to help the captain, nor any | treasureisland
other thought but that he had got his death-hurt in the scuffle with | treasureisland
the stranger. I got the rum, to be sure, and tried to put it down his | treasureisland
throat, but his teeth were tightly shut and his jaws as strong as iron. | treasureisland
It was a happy relief for us when the door opened and Doctor Livesey | treasureisland
came in, on his visit to my father. | treasureisland
“Oh, doctor,” we cried, “what shall we do? Where is he wounded?” | treasureisland
“Wounded? A fiddle-stick's end!” said the doctor. “No more wounded than | treasureisland
you or I. The man has had a stroke, as I warned him. Now, Mrs. Hawkins, | treasureisland
just you run upstairs to your husband and tell him, if possible, nothing | treasureisland
about it. For my part, I must do my best to save this fellow's trebly | treasureisland
worthless life; Jim, you get me a basin.” | treasureisland
When I got back with the basin, the doctor had already ripped up the | treasureisland
captain's sleeve and exposed his great sinewy arm. It was tattooed | treasureisland
in several places. “Here's luck,” “A fair wind,” and “Billy Bones his | treasureisland
fancy,” were very neatly and clearly executed on the forearm; and up | treasureisland
near the shoulder there was a sketch of a gallows and a man hanging from | treasureisland
it--done, as I thought, with great spirit. | treasureisland
“Prophetic,” said the doctor, touching this picture with his finger. | treasureisland
“And now, Master Billy Bones, if that be your name, we'll have a look at | treasureisland
the colour of your blood. Jim,” he said, “are you afraid of blood?” | treasureisland
“No, sir,” said I. | treasureisland
“Well, then,” said he, “you hold the basin”; and with that he took his | treasureisland
lancet and opened a vein. | treasureisland
A great deal of blood was taken before the captain opened his eyes | treasureisland
and looked mistily about him. First he recognized the doctor with | treasureisland
an unmistakable frown; then his glance fell upon me, and he looked | treasureisland
relieved. But suddenly his colour changed, and he tried to raise | treasureisland
himself, crying, “Where's Black Dog?” | treasureisland
“There is no Black Dog here,” said the doctor, “except what you have | treasureisland
on your own back. You have been drinking rum; you have had a stroke, | treasureisland
precisely as I told you; and I have just, very much against my own will, | treasureisland
dragged you headforemost out of the grave. Now, Mr. Bones--” | treasureisland
“That's not my name,” he interrupted. | treasureisland
“Much I care,” returned the doctor. “It's the name of a buccaneer of my | treasureisland
acquaintance; and I call you by it for the sake of shortness, and what I | treasureisland
have to say to you is this; one glass of rum won't kill you, but if | treasureisland
you take one you'll take another and another, and I stake my wig if you | treasureisland
don't break off short, you'll die--do you understand that?--die, and go | treasureisland
to your own place, like the man in the Bible. Come, now, make an effort. | treasureisland
I'll help you to your bed for once.” | treasureisland
Between us, with much trouble, we managed to hoist him upstairs, and | treasureisland
laid him on his bed, where his head fell back on the pillow as if he | treasureisland
were almost fainting. | treasureisland
“Now, mind you,” said the doctor, “I clear my conscience--the name of | treasureisland
rum for you is death.” | treasureisland
And with that he went off to see my father, taking me with him by the | treasureisland
arm. | treasureisland
“This is nothing,” he said as soon as he had closed the door. “I have | treasureisland
drawn blood enough to keep him quiet awhile; he should lie for a week | treasureisland
where he is--that is the best thing for him and you; but another stroke | treasureisland
would settle him.” | treasureisland
3 | treasureisland
The Black Spot | treasureisland
ABOUT noon I stopped at the captain's door with some cooling drinks | treasureisland
and medicines. He was lying very much as we had left him, only a little | treasureisland
higher, and he seemed both weak and excited. | treasureisland
“Jim,” he said, “you're the only one here that's worth anything, and you | treasureisland
know I've been always good to you. Never a month but I've given you a | treasureisland
silver fourpenny for yourself. And now you see, mate, I'm pretty low, | treasureisland
and deserted by all; and Jim, you'll bring me one noggin of rum, now, | treasureisland
won't you, matey?” | treasureisland
“The doctor--” I began. | treasureisland
But he broke in cursing the doctor, in a feeble voice but heartily. | treasureisland
“Doctors is all swabs,” he said; “and that doctor there, why, what do | treasureisland
he know about seafaring men? I been in places hot as pitch, and mates | treasureisland
dropping round with Yellow Jack, and the blessed land a-heaving like the | treasureisland
sea with earthquakes--what to the doctor know of lands like that?--and I | treasureisland
lived on rum, I tell you. It's been meat and drink, and man and wife, | treasureisland
to me; and if I'm not to have my rum now I'm a poor old hulk on a lee | treasureisland
shore, my blood'll be on you, Jim, and that doctor swab”; and he ran on | treasureisland
again for a while with curses. “Look, Jim, how my fingers fidges,” | treasureisland
 he continued in the pleading tone. “I can't keep 'em still, not I. I | treasureisland
haven't had a drop this blessed day. That doctor's a fool, I tell you. | treasureisland
If I don't have a drain o' rum, Jim, I'll have the horrors; I seen some | treasureisland
on 'em already. I seen old Flint in the corner there, behind you; as | treasureisland
plain as print, I seen him; and if I get the horrors, I'm a man that | treasureisland
has lived rough, and I'll raise Cain. Your doctor hisself said one glass | treasureisland
wouldn't hurt me. I'll give you a golden guinea for a noggin, Jim.” | treasureisland
He was growing more and more excited, and this alarmed me for my father, | treasureisland
who was very low that day and needed quiet; besides, I was reassured by | treasureisland
the doctor's words, now quoted to me, and rather offended by the offer | treasureisland
of a bribe. | treasureisland
“I want none of your money,” said I, “but what you owe my father. I'll | treasureisland
get you one glass, and no more.” | treasureisland
When I brought it to him, he seized it greedily and drank it out. | treasureisland
“Aye, aye,” said he, “that's some better, sure enough. And now, matey, | treasureisland
did that doctor say how long I was to lie here in this old berth?” | treasureisland
“A week at least,” said I. | treasureisland
“Thunder!” he cried. “A week! I can't do that; they'd have the black | treasureisland
spot on me by then. The lubbers is going about to get the wind of me | treasureisland
this blessed moment; lubbers as couldn't keep what they got, and want to | treasureisland
nail what is another's. Is that seamanly behaviour, now, I want to know? | treasureisland
But I'm a saving soul. I never wasted good money of mine, nor lost it | treasureisland
neither; and I'll trick 'em again. I'm not afraid on 'em. I'll shake out | treasureisland
another reef, matey, and daddle 'em again.” | treasureisland
As he was thus speaking, he had risen from bed with great difficulty, | treasureisland
holding to my shoulder with a grip that almost made me cry out, and | treasureisland
moving his legs like so much dead weight. His words, spirited as they | treasureisland
were in meaning, contrasted sadly with the weakness of the voice in | treasureisland
which they were uttered. He paused when he had got into a sitting | treasureisland
position on the edge. | treasureisland
“That doctor's done me,” he murmured. “My ears is singing. Lay me back.” | treasureisland
Before I could do much to help him he had fallen back again to his | treasureisland
former place, where he lay for a while silent. | treasureisland
“Jim,” he said at length, “you saw that seafaring man today?” | treasureisland
“Black Dog?” I asked. | treasureisland
“Ah! Black Dog,” says he. “HE'S a bad un; but there's worse that put him | treasureisland
on. Now, if I can't get away nohow, and they tip me the black spot, mind | treasureisland
you, it's my old sea-chest they're after; you get on a horse--you can, | treasureisland
can't you? Well, then, you get on a horse, and go to--well, yes, | treasureisland
I will!--to that eternal doctor swab, and tell him to pipe all | treasureisland
hands--magistrates and sich--and he'll lay 'em aboard at the Admiral | treasureisland
Benbow--all old Flint's crew, man and boy, all on 'em that's left. I was | treasureisland
first mate, I was, old Flint's first mate, and I'm the on'y one as knows | treasureisland
the place. He gave it me at Savannah, when he lay a-dying, like as if I | treasureisland
was to now, you see. But you won't peach unless they get the black spot | treasureisland
on me, or unless you see that Black Dog again or a seafaring man with | treasureisland
one leg, Jim--him above all.” | treasureisland
“But what is the black spot, captain?” I asked. | treasureisland
“That's a summons, mate. I'll tell you if they get that. But you keep | treasureisland
your weather-eye open, Jim, and I'll share with you equals, upon my | treasureisland
honour.” | treasureisland
He wandered a little longer, his voice growing weaker; but soon after I | treasureisland
had given him his medicine, which he took like a child, with the remark, | treasureisland
“If ever a seaman wanted drugs, it's me,” he fell at last into a heavy, | treasureisland
swoon-like sleep, in which I left him. What I should have done had all | treasureisland
gone well I do not know. Probably I should have told the whole story to | treasureisland
the doctor, for I was in mortal fear lest the captain should repent of | treasureisland
his confessions and make an end of me. But as things fell out, my poor | treasureisland
father died quite suddenly that evening, which put all other matters | treasureisland
on one side. Our natural distress, the visits of the neighbours, the | treasureisland
arranging of the funeral, and all the work of the inn to be carried on | treasureisland
in the meanwhile kept me so busy that I had scarcely time to think of | treasureisland
the captain, far less to be afraid of him. | treasureisland
He got downstairs next morning, to be sure, and had his meals as usual, | treasureisland
though he ate little and had more, I am afraid, than his usual supply of | treasureisland
rum, for he helped himself out of the bar, scowling and blowing through | treasureisland
his nose, and no one dared to cross him. On the night before the funeral | treasureisland
he was as drunk as ever; and it was shocking, in that house of mourning, | treasureisland
to hear him singing away at his ugly old sea-song; but weak as he was, | treasureisland
we were all in the fear of death for him, and the doctor was suddenly | treasureisland
taken up with a case many miles away and was never near the house after | treasureisland
my father's death. I have said the captain was weak, and indeed he | treasureisland
seemed rather to grow weaker than regain his strength. He clambered up | treasureisland
and down stairs, and went from the parlour to the bar and back again, | treasureisland
and sometimes put his nose out of doors to smell the sea, holding on to | treasureisland
the walls as he went for support and breathing hard and fast like a man | treasureisland
on a steep mountain. He never particularly addressed me, and it is my | treasureisland
belief he had as good as forgotten his confidences; but his temper was | treasureisland
more flighty, and allowing for his bodily weakness, more violent than | treasureisland
ever. He had an alarming way now when he was drunk of drawing his | treasureisland
cutlass and laying it bare before him on the table. But with all that, | treasureisland
he minded people less and seemed shut up in his own thoughts and rather | treasureisland
wandering. Once, for instance, to our extreme wonder, he piped up to a | treasureisland
different air, a kind of country love-song that he must have learned in | treasureisland
his youth before he had begun to follow the sea. | treasureisland
So things passed until, the day after the funeral, and about three | treasureisland
o'clock of a bitter, foggy, frosty afternoon, I was standing at the door | treasureisland
for a moment, full of sad thoughts about my father, when I saw someone | treasureisland
drawing slowly near along the road. He was plainly blind, for he tapped | treasureisland
before him with a stick and wore a great green shade over his eyes and | treasureisland
nose; and he was hunched, as if with age or weakness, and wore a huge | treasureisland
old tattered sea-cloak with a hood that made him appear positively | treasureisland
deformed. I never saw in my life a more dreadful-looking figure. | treasureisland
He stopped a little from the inn, and raising his voice in an odd | treasureisland
sing-song, addressed the air in front of him, “Will any kind friend | treasureisland
inform a poor blind man, who has lost the precious sight of his eyes in | treasureisland
the gracious defence of his native country, England--and God bless King | treasureisland
George!--where or in what part of this country he may now be?” | treasureisland
“You are at the Admiral Benbow, Black Hill Cove, my good man,” said I. | treasureisland
“I hear a voice,” said he, “a young voice. Will you give me your hand, | treasureisland
my kind young friend, and lead me in?” | treasureisland
I held out my hand, and the horrible, soft-spoken, eyeless creature | treasureisland
gripped it in a moment like a vise. I was so much startled that I | treasureisland
struggled to withdraw, but the blind man pulled me close up to him with | treasureisland
a single action of his arm. | treasureisland
“Now, boy,” he said, “take me in to the captain.” | treasureisland
“Sir,” said I, “upon my word I dare not.” | treasureisland
“Oh,” he sneered, “that's it! Take me in straight or I'll break your | treasureisland
arm.” | treasureisland
And he gave it, as he spoke, a wrench that made me cry out. | treasureisland
“Sir,” said I, “it is for yourself I mean. The captain is not what he | treasureisland
used to be. He sits with a drawn cutlass. Another gentleman--” | treasureisland
“Come, now, march,” interrupted he; and I never heard a voice so cruel, | treasureisland
and cold, and ugly as that blind man's. It cowed me more than the pain, | treasureisland
and I began to obey him at once, walking straight in at the door and | treasureisland
towards the parlour, where our sick old buccaneer was sitting, dazed | treasureisland
with rum. The blind man clung close to me, holding me in one iron fist | treasureisland
and leaning almost more of his weight on me than I could carry. “Lead me | treasureisland
straight up to him, and when I'm in view, cry out, 'Here's a friend | treasureisland
for you, Bill.' If you don't, I'll do this,” and with that he gave me a | treasureisland
twitch that I thought would have made me faint. Between this and that, I | treasureisland
was so utterly terrified of the blind beggar that I forgot my terror of | treasureisland
the captain, and as I opened the parlour door, cried out the words he | treasureisland
had ordered in a trembling voice. | treasureisland
The poor captain raised his eyes, and at one look the rum went out of | treasureisland
him and left him staring sober. The expression of his face was not so | treasureisland
much of terror as of mortal sickness. He made a movement to rise, but I | treasureisland
do not believe he had enough force left in his body. | treasureisland
“Now, Bill, sit where you are,” said the beggar. “If I can't see, I can | treasureisland
hear a finger stirring. Business is business. Hold out your left hand. | treasureisland
Boy, take his left hand by the wrist and bring it near to my right.” | treasureisland
We both obeyed him to the letter, and I saw him pass something from the | treasureisland
hollow of the hand that held his stick into the palm of the captain's, | treasureisland
which closed upon it instantly. | treasureisland
“And now that's done,” said the blind man; and at the words he suddenly | treasureisland
left hold of me, and with incredible accuracy and nimbleness, | treasureisland
skipped out of the parlour and into the road, where, as I still stood | treasureisland
motionless, I could hear his stick go tap-tap-tapping into the distance. | treasureisland
It was some time before either I or the captain seemed to gather our | treasureisland
senses, but at length, and about at the same moment, I released his | treasureisland
wrist, which I was still holding, and he drew in his hand and looked | treasureisland
sharply into the palm. | treasureisland
“Ten o'clock!” he cried. “Six hours. We'll do them yet,” and he sprang | treasureisland
to his feet. | treasureisland
Even as he did so, he reeled, put his hand to his throat, stood swaying | treasureisland
for a moment, and then, with a peculiar sound, fell from his whole | treasureisland
height face foremost to the floor. | treasureisland
I ran to him at once, calling to my mother. But haste was all in vain. | treasureisland
The captain had been struck dead by thundering apoplexy. It is a curious | treasureisland
thing to understand, for I had certainly never liked the man, though of | treasureisland
late I had begun to pity him, but as soon as I saw that he was dead, I | treasureisland
burst into a flood of tears. It was the second death I had known, and | treasureisland
the sorrow of the first was still fresh in my heart. | treasureisland
4 | treasureisland
The Sea-chest | treasureisland
I LOST no time, of course, in telling my mother all that I knew, and | treasureisland
perhaps should have told her long before, and we saw ourselves at once | treasureisland
in a difficult and dangerous position. Some of the man's money--if | treasureisland
he had any--was certainly due to us, but it was not likely that our | treasureisland
captain's shipmates, above all the two specimens seen by me, Black | treasureisland
Dog and the blind beggar, would be inclined to give up their booty in | treasureisland
payment of the dead man's debts. The captain's order to mount at | treasureisland
once and ride for Doctor Livesey would have left my mother alone | treasureisland
and unprotected, which was not to be thought of. Indeed, it seemed | treasureisland
impossible for either of us to remain much longer in the house; the fall | treasureisland
of coals in the kitchen grate, the very ticking of the clock, filled | treasureisland
us with alarms. The neighbourhood, to our ears, seemed haunted by | treasureisland
approaching footsteps; and what between the dead body of the captain | treasureisland
on the parlour floor and the thought of that detestable blind beggar | treasureisland
hovering near at hand and ready to return, there were moments when, as | treasureisland
the saying goes, I jumped in my skin for terror. Something must speedily | treasureisland
be resolved upon, and it occurred to us at last to go forth together | treasureisland
and seek help in the neighbouring hamlet. No sooner said than done. | treasureisland
Bare-headed as we were, we ran out at once in the gathering evening and | treasureisland
the frosty fog. | treasureisland
The hamlet lay not many hundred yards away, though out of view, on the | treasureisland
other side of the next cove; and what greatly encouraged me, it was | treasureisland
in an opposite direction from that whence the blind man had made his | treasureisland
appearance and whither he had presumably returned. We were not many | treasureisland
minutes on the road, though we sometimes stopped to lay hold of each | treasureisland
other and hearken. But there was no unusual sound--nothing but the low | treasureisland
wash of the ripple and the croaking of the inmates of the wood. | treasureisland
It was already candle-light when we reached the hamlet, and I shall | treasureisland
never forget how much I was cheered to see the yellow shine in doors and | treasureisland
windows; but that, as it proved, was the best of the help we were likely | treasureisland
to get in that quarter. For--you would have thought men would have been | treasureisland
ashamed of themselves--no soul would consent to return with us to the | treasureisland
Admiral Benbow. The more we told of our troubles, the more--man, woman, | treasureisland
and child--they clung to the shelter of their houses. The name of | treasureisland
Captain Flint, though it was strange to me, was well enough known to | treasureisland
some there and carried a great weight of terror. Some of the men who | treasureisland
had been to field-work on the far side of the Admiral Benbow remembered, | treasureisland
besides, to have seen several strangers on the road, and taking them to | treasureisland
be smugglers, to have bolted away; and one at least had seen a little | treasureisland
lugger in what we called Kitt's Hole. For that matter, anyone who was a | treasureisland
comrade of the captain's was enough to frighten them to death. And the | treasureisland
short and the long of the matter was, that while we could get several | treasureisland
who were willing enough to ride to Dr. Livesey's, which lay in another | treasureisland
direction, not one would help us to defend the inn. | treasureisland
They say cowardice is infectious; but then argument is, on the other | treasureisland
hand, a great emboldener; and so when each had said his say, my mother | treasureisland
made them a speech. She would not, she declared, lose money that | treasureisland
belonged to her fatherless boy; “If none of the rest of you dare,” | treasureisland
 she said, “Jim and I dare. Back we will go, the way we came, and small | treasureisland
thanks to you big, hulking, chicken-hearted men. We'll have that chest | treasureisland
open, if we die for it. And I'll thank you for that bag, Mrs. Crossley, | treasureisland
to bring back our lawful money in.” | treasureisland
Of course I said I would go with my mother, and of course they all cried | treasureisland
out at our foolhardiness, but even then not a man would go along with | treasureisland
us. All they would do was to give me a loaded pistol lest we were | treasureisland
attacked, and to promise to have horses ready saddled in case we were | treasureisland
pursued on our return, while one lad was to ride forward to the doctor's | treasureisland
in search of armed assistance. | treasureisland
My heart was beating finely when we two set forth in the cold night upon | treasureisland
this dangerous venture. A full moon was beginning to rise and peered | treasureisland
redly through the upper edges of the fog, and this increased our haste, | treasureisland
for it was plain, before we came forth again, that all would be as | treasureisland
bright as day, and our departure exposed to the eyes of any watchers. | treasureisland
We slipped along the hedges, noiseless and swift, nor did we see or hear | treasureisland
anything to increase our terrors, till, to our relief, the door of the | treasureisland
Admiral Benbow had closed behind us. | treasureisland
I slipped the bolt at once, and we stood and panted for a moment in the | treasureisland
dark, alone in the house with the dead captain's body. Then my mother | treasureisland
got a candle in the bar, and holding each other's hands, we advanced | treasureisland
into the parlour. He lay as we had left him, on his back, with his eyes | treasureisland
open and one arm stretched out. | treasureisland
“Draw down the blind, Jim,” whispered my mother; “they might come and | treasureisland
watch outside. And now,” said she when I had done so, “we have to get | treasureisland
the key off THAT; and who's to touch it, I should like to know!” and she | treasureisland
gave a kind of sob as she said the words. | treasureisland
I went down on my knees at once. On the floor close to his hand there | treasureisland
was a little round of paper, blackened on the one side. I could not | treasureisland
doubt that this was the BLACK SPOT; and taking it up, I found written | treasureisland
on the other side, in a very good, clear hand, this short message: “You | treasureisland
have till ten tonight.” | treasureisland
“He had till ten, Mother,” said I; and just as I said it, our old clock | treasureisland
began striking. This sudden noise startled us shockingly; but the news | treasureisland
was good, for it was only six. | treasureisland
“Now, Jim,” she said, “that key.” | treasureisland
I felt in his pockets, one after another. A few small coins, a thimble, | treasureisland
and some thread and big needles, a piece of pigtail tobacco bitten away | treasureisland
at the end, his gully with the crooked handle, a pocket compass, and a | treasureisland
tinder box were all that they contained, and I began to despair. | treasureisland
“Perhaps it's round his neck,” suggested my mother. | treasureisland
Overcoming a strong repugnance, I tore open his shirt at the neck, and | treasureisland
there, sure enough, hanging to a bit of tarry string, which I cut with | treasureisland
his own gully, we found the key. At this triumph we were filled with | treasureisland
hope and hurried upstairs without delay to the little room where he had | treasureisland
slept so long and where his box had stood since the day of his arrival. | treasureisland
It was like any other seaman's chest on the outside, the initial “B” | treasureisland
 burned on the top of it with a hot iron, and the corners somewhat | treasureisland
smashed and broken as by long, rough usage. | treasureisland
“Give me the key,” said my mother; and though the lock was very stiff, | treasureisland
she had turned it and thrown back the lid in a twinkling. | treasureisland
A strong smell of tobacco and tar rose from the interior, but nothing | treasureisland
was to be seen on the top except a suit of very good clothes, carefully | treasureisland
brushed and folded. They had never been worn, my mother said. Under | treasureisland
that, the miscellany began--a quadrant, a tin canikin, several sticks of | treasureisland
tobacco, two brace of very handsome pistols, a piece of bar silver, an | treasureisland
old Spanish watch and some other trinkets of little value and mostly of | treasureisland
foreign make, a pair of compasses mounted with brass, and five or six | treasureisland
curious West Indian shells. I have often wondered since why he should | treasureisland
have carried about these shells with him in his wandering, guilty, and | treasureisland
hunted life. | treasureisland
In the meantime, we had found nothing of any value but the silver and | treasureisland
the trinkets, and neither of these were in our way. Underneath there | treasureisland
was an old boat-cloak, whitened with sea-salt on many a harbour-bar. My | treasureisland
mother pulled it up with impatience, and there lay before us, the last | treasureisland
things in the chest, a bundle tied up in oilcloth, and looking like | treasureisland
papers, and a canvas bag that gave forth, at a touch, the jingle of | treasureisland
gold. | treasureisland
“I'll show these rogues that I'm an honest woman,” said my mother. “I'll | treasureisland
have my dues, and not a farthing over. Hold Mrs. Crossley's bag.” And | treasureisland
she began to count over the amount of the captain's score from the | treasureisland
sailor's bag into the one that I was holding. | treasureisland
It was a long, difficult business, for the coins were of all countries | treasureisland
and sizes--doubloons, and louis d'ors, and guineas, and pieces of eight, | treasureisland
and I know not what besides, all shaken together at random. The guineas, | treasureisland
too, were about the scarcest, and it was with these only that my mother | treasureisland
knew how to make her count. | treasureisland
When we were about half-way through, I suddenly put my hand upon her | treasureisland
arm, for I had heard in the silent frosty air a sound that brought my | treasureisland
heart into my mouth--the tap-tapping of the blind man's stick upon the | treasureisland
frozen road. It drew nearer and nearer, while we sat holding our breath. | treasureisland
Then it struck sharp on the inn door, and then we could hear the handle | treasureisland
being turned and the bolt rattling as the wretched being tried to enter; | treasureisland
and then there was a long time of silence both within and without. | treasureisland
At last the tapping recommenced, and, to our indescribable joy and | treasureisland
gratitude, died slowly away again until it ceased to be heard. | treasureisland
“Mother,” said I, “take the whole and let's be going,” for I was sure | treasureisland
the bolted door must have seemed suspicious and would bring the whole | treasureisland
hornet's nest about our ears, though how thankful I was that I had | treasureisland
bolted it, none could tell who had never met that terrible blind man. | treasureisland
But my mother, frightened as she was, would not consent to take a | treasureisland
fraction more than was due to her and was obstinately unwilling to be | treasureisland
content with less. It was not yet seven, she said, by a long way; she | treasureisland
knew her rights and she would have them; and she was still arguing with | treasureisland
me when a little low whistle sounded a good way off upon the hill. That | treasureisland
was enough, and more than enough, for both of us. | treasureisland
“I'll take what I have,” she said, jumping to her feet. | treasureisland
“And I'll take this to square the count,” said I, picking up the oilskin | treasureisland
packet. | treasureisland
Next moment we were both groping downstairs, leaving the candle by | treasureisland
the empty chest; and the next we had opened the door and were in full | treasureisland
retreat. We had not started a moment too soon. The fog was rapidly | treasureisland
dispersing; already the moon shone quite clear on the high ground on | treasureisland
either side; and it was only in the exact bottom of the dell and round | treasureisland
the tavern door that a thin veil still hung unbroken to conceal the | treasureisland
first steps of our escape. Far less than half-way to the hamlet, very | treasureisland
little beyond the bottom of the hill, we must come forth into the | treasureisland
moonlight. Nor was this all, for the sound of several footsteps running | treasureisland
came already to our ears, and as we looked back in their direction, a | treasureisland
light tossing to and fro and still rapidly advancing showed that one of | treasureisland
the newcomers carried a lantern. | treasureisland
“My dear,” said my mother suddenly, “take the money and run on. I am | treasureisland
going to faint.” | treasureisland
This was certainly the end for both of us, I thought. How I cursed the | treasureisland
cowardice of the neighbours; how I blamed my poor mother for her honesty | treasureisland
and her greed, for her past foolhardiness and present weakness! We were | treasureisland
just at the little bridge, by good fortune; and I helped her, tottering | treasureisland
as she was, to the edge of the bank, where, sure enough, she gave a sigh | treasureisland
and fell on my shoulder. I do not know how I found the strength to do it | treasureisland
at all, and I am afraid it was roughly done, but I managed to drag her | treasureisland
down the bank and a little way under the arch. Farther I could not move | treasureisland
her, for the bridge was too low to let me do more than crawl below it. | treasureisland
So there we had to stay--my mother almost entirely exposed and both of | treasureisland
us within earshot of the inn. | treasureisland
5 | treasureisland
The Last of the Blind Man | treasureisland
MY curiosity, in a sense, was stronger than my fear, for I could not | treasureisland
remain where I was, but crept back to the bank again, whence, sheltering | treasureisland
my head behind a bush of broom, I might command the road before our | treasureisland
door. I was scarcely in position ere my enemies began to arrive, seven | treasureisland
or eight of them, running hard, their feet beating out of time along | treasureisland
the road and the man with the lantern some paces in front. Three men ran | treasureisland
together, hand in hand; and I made out, even through the mist, that the | treasureisland
middle man of this trio was the blind beggar. The next moment his voice | treasureisland
showed me that I was right. | treasureisland
“Down with the door!” he cried. | treasureisland
“Aye, aye, sir!” answered two or three; and a rush was made upon the | treasureisland
Admiral Benbow, the lantern-bearer following; and then I could see | treasureisland
them pause, and hear speeches passed in a lower key, as if they were | treasureisland
surprised to find the door open. But the pause was brief, for the blind | treasureisland
man again issued his commands. His voice sounded louder and higher, as | treasureisland
if he were afire with eagerness and rage. | treasureisland
“In, in, in!” he shouted, and cursed them for their delay. | treasureisland
Four or five of them obeyed at once, two remaining on the road with the | treasureisland
formidable beggar. There was a pause, then a cry of surprise, and then a | treasureisland
voice shouting from the house, “Bill's dead.” | treasureisland
But the blind man swore at them again for their delay. | treasureisland
“Search him, some of you shirking lubbers, and the rest of you aloft and | treasureisland
get the chest,” he cried. | treasureisland
I could hear their feet rattling up our old stairs, so that the | treasureisland
house must have shook with it. Promptly afterwards, fresh sounds of | treasureisland
astonishment arose; the window of the captain's room was thrown open | treasureisland
with a slam and a jingle of broken glass, and a man leaned out into the | treasureisland
moonlight, head and shoulders, and addressed the blind beggar on the | treasureisland
road below him. | treasureisland
“Pew,” he cried, “they've been before us. Someone's turned the chest out | treasureisland
alow and aloft.” | treasureisland
“Is it there?” roared Pew. | treasureisland
“The money's there.” | treasureisland
The blind man cursed the money. | treasureisland
“Flint's fist, I mean,” he cried. | treasureisland
“We don't see it here nohow,” returned the man. | treasureisland
“Here, you below there, is it on Bill?” cried the blind man again. | treasureisland
At that another fellow, probably him who had remained below to search | treasureisland
the captain's body, came to the door of the inn. “Bill's been overhauled | treasureisland
a'ready,” said he; “nothin' left.” | treasureisland
“It's these people of the inn--it's that boy. I wish I had put his eyes | treasureisland
out!” cried the blind man, Pew. “There were no time ago--they had the | treasureisland
door bolted when I tried it. Scatter, lads, and find 'em.” | treasureisland
“Sure enough, they left their glim here,” said the fellow from the | treasureisland
window. | treasureisland
“Scatter and find 'em! Rout the house out!” reiterated Pew, striking | treasureisland
with his stick upon the road. | treasureisland
Then there followed a great to-do through all our old inn, heavy feet | treasureisland
pounding to and fro, furniture thrown over, doors kicked in, until the | treasureisland
very rocks re-echoed and the men came out again, one after another, on | treasureisland
the road and declared that we were nowhere to be found. And just | treasureisland
the same whistle that had alarmed my mother and myself over the dead | treasureisland
captain's money was once more clearly audible through the night, | treasureisland
but this time twice repeated. I had thought it to be the blind man's | treasureisland
trumpet, so to speak, summoning his crew to the assault, but I now found | treasureisland
that it was a signal from the hillside towards the hamlet, and from its | treasureisland
effect upon the buccaneers, a signal to warn them of approaching danger. | treasureisland
“There's Dirk again,” said one. “Twice! We'll have to budge, mates.” | treasureisland
“Budge, you skulk!” cried Pew. “Dirk was a fool and a coward from the | treasureisland
first--you wouldn't mind him. They must be close by; they can't be far; | treasureisland
you have your hands on it. Scatter and look for them, dogs! Oh, shiver | treasureisland
my soul,” he cried, “if I had eyes!” | treasureisland
This appeal seemed to produce some effect, for two of the fellows began | treasureisland
to look here and there among the lumber, but half-heartedly, I thought, | treasureisland
and with half an eye to their own danger all the time, while the rest | treasureisland
stood irresolute on the road. | treasureisland
“You have your hands on thousands, you fools, and you hang a leg! You'd | treasureisland
be as rich as kings if you could find it, and you know it's here, and | treasureisland
you stand there skulking. There wasn't one of you dared face Bill, and | treasureisland
I did it--a blind man! And I'm to lose my chance for you! I'm to be a | treasureisland
poor, crawling beggar, sponging for rum, when I might be rolling in a | treasureisland
coach! If you had the pluck of a weevil in a biscuit you would catch | treasureisland
them still.” | treasureisland
“Hang it, Pew, we've got the doubloons!” grumbled one. | treasureisland
“They might have hid the blessed thing,” said another. “Take the | treasureisland
Georges, Pew, and don't stand here squalling.” | treasureisland
Squalling was the word for it; Pew's anger rose so high at these | treasureisland
objections till at last, his passion completely taking the upper hand, | treasureisland
he struck at them right and left in his blindness and his stick sounded | treasureisland
heavily on more than one. | treasureisland
These, in their turn, cursed back at the blind miscreant, threatened him | treasureisland
in horrid terms, and tried in vain to catch the stick and wrest it from | treasureisland
his grasp. | treasureisland
This quarrel was the saving of us, for while it was still raging, | treasureisland
another sound came from the top of the hill on the side of the | treasureisland
hamlet--the tramp of horses galloping. Almost at the same time a | treasureisland
pistol-shot, flash and report, came from the hedge side. And that was | treasureisland
plainly the last signal of danger, for the buccaneers turned at once | treasureisland
and ran, separating in every direction, one seaward along the cove, one | treasureisland
slant across the hill, and so on, so that in half a minute not a sign of | treasureisland
them remained but Pew. Him they had deserted, whether in sheer panic | treasureisland
or out of revenge for his ill words and blows I know not; but there he | treasureisland
remained behind, tapping up and down the road in a frenzy, and groping | treasureisland
and calling for his comrades. Finally he took a wrong turn and ran a few | treasureisland
steps past me, towards the hamlet, crying, “Johnny, Black Dog, Dirk,” | treasureisland
 and other names, “you won't leave old Pew, mates--not old Pew!” | treasureisland
Just then the noise of horses topped the rise, and four or five riders | treasureisland
came in sight in the moonlight and swept at full gallop down the slope. | treasureisland
At this Pew saw his error, turned with a scream, and ran straight for | treasureisland
the ditch, into which he rolled. But he was on his feet again in a | treasureisland
second and made another dash, now utterly bewildered, right under the | treasureisland
nearest of the coming horses. | treasureisland
The rider tried to save him, but in vain. Down went Pew with a cry that | treasureisland
rang high into the night; and the four hoofs trampled and spurned him | treasureisland
and passed by. He fell on his side, then gently collapsed upon his face | treasureisland
and moved no more. | treasureisland
I leaped to my feet and hailed the riders. They were pulling up, at any | treasureisland
rate, horrified at the accident; and I soon saw what they were. One, | treasureisland
tailing out behind the rest, was a lad that had gone from the hamlet to | treasureisland
Dr. Livesey's; the rest were revenue officers, whom he had met by the | treasureisland
way, and with whom he had had the intelligence to return at once. Some | treasureisland
news of the lugger in Kitt's Hole had found its way to Supervisor Dance | treasureisland
and set him forth that night in our direction, and to that circumstance | treasureisland
my mother and I owed our preservation from death. | treasureisland
Pew was dead, stone dead. As for my mother, when we had carried her up | treasureisland
to the hamlet, a little cold water and salts and that soon brought her | treasureisland
back again, and she was none the worse for her terror, though she still | treasureisland
continued to deplore the balance of the money. In the meantime the | treasureisland
supervisor rode on, as fast as he could, to Kitt's Hole; but his men | treasureisland
had to dismount and grope down the dingle, leading, and sometimes | treasureisland
supporting, their horses, and in continual fear of ambushes; so it was | treasureisland
no great matter for surprise that when they got down to the Hole the | treasureisland
lugger was already under way, though still close in. He hailed her. A | treasureisland
voice replied, telling him to keep out of the moonlight or he would get | treasureisland
some lead in him, and at the same time a bullet whistled close by his | treasureisland
arm. Soon after, the lugger doubled the point and disappeared. Mr. Dance | treasureisland
stood there, as he said, “like a fish out of water,” and all he could do | treasureisland
was to dispatch a man to B---- to warn the cutter. “And that,” said he, | treasureisland
“is just about as good as nothing. They've got off clean, and there's | treasureisland
an end. Only,” he added, “I'm glad I trod on Master Pew's corns,” for by | treasureisland
this time he had heard my story. | treasureisland
I went back with him to the Admiral Benbow, and you cannot imagine a | treasureisland
house in such a state of smash; the very clock had been thrown down | treasureisland
by these fellows in their furious hunt after my mother and myself; | treasureisland
and though nothing had actually been taken away except the captain's | treasureisland
money-bag and a little silver from the till, I could see at once that we | treasureisland
were ruined. Mr. Dance could make nothing of the scene. | treasureisland
“They got the money, you say? Well, then, Hawkins, what in fortune were | treasureisland
they after? More money, I suppose?” | treasureisland
“No, sir; not money, I think,” replied I. “In fact, sir, I believe I | treasureisland
have the thing in my breast pocket; and to tell you the truth, I should | treasureisland
like to get it put in safety.” | treasureisland
“To be sure, boy; quite right,” said he. “I'll take it, if you like.” | treasureisland
“I thought perhaps Dr. Livesey--” I began. | treasureisland
“Perfectly right,” he interrupted very cheerily, “perfectly right--a | treasureisland
gentleman and a magistrate. And, now I come to think of it, I might as | treasureisland
well ride round there myself and report to him or squire. Master Pew's | treasureisland
dead, when all's done; not that I regret it, but he's dead, you see, and | treasureisland
people will make it out against an officer of his Majesty's revenue, | treasureisland
if make it out they can. Now, I'll tell you, Hawkins, if you like, I'll | treasureisland
take you along.” | treasureisland
I thanked him heartily for the offer, and we walked back to the hamlet | treasureisland
where the horses were. By the time I had told mother of my purpose they | treasureisland
were all in the saddle. | treasureisland
“Dogger,” said Mr. Dance, “you have a good horse; take up this lad | treasureisland
behind you.” | treasureisland
As soon as I was mounted, holding on to Dogger's belt, the supervisor | treasureisland
gave the word, and the party struck out at a bouncing trot on the road | treasureisland
to Dr. Livesey's house. | treasureisland
6 | treasureisland
The Captain's Papers | treasureisland
WE rode hard all the way till we drew up before Dr. Livesey's door. The | treasureisland
house was all dark to the front. | treasureisland
Mr. Dance told me to jump down and knock, and Dogger gave me a stirrup | treasureisland
to descend by. The door was opened almost at once by the maid. | treasureisland
“Is Dr. Livesey in?” I asked. | treasureisland
No, she said, he had come home in the afternoon but had gone up to the | treasureisland
hall to dine and pass the evening with the squire. | treasureisland
“So there we go, boys,” said Mr. Dance. | treasureisland
This time, as the distance was short, I did not mount, but ran with | treasureisland
Dogger's stirrup-leather to the lodge gates and up the long, leafless, | treasureisland
moonlit avenue to where the white line of the hall buildings looked on | treasureisland
either hand on great old gardens. Here Mr. Dance dismounted, and taking | treasureisland
me along with him, was admitted at a word into the house. | treasureisland
The servant led us down a matted passage and showed us at the end into a | treasureisland
great library, all lined with bookcases and busts upon the top of them, | treasureisland
where the squire and Dr. Livesey sat, pipe in hand, on either side of a | treasureisland
bright fire. | treasureisland
I had never seen the squire so near at hand. He was a tall man, over six | treasureisland
feet high, and broad in proportion, and he had a bluff, rough-and-ready | treasureisland
face, all roughened and reddened and lined in his long travels. His | treasureisland
eyebrows were very black, and moved readily, and this gave him a look of | treasureisland
some temper, not bad, you would say, but quick and high. | treasureisland
“Come in, Mr. Dance,” says he, very stately and condescending. | treasureisland
“Good evening, Dance,” says the doctor with a nod. “And good evening to | treasureisland
you, friend Jim. What good wind brings you here?” | treasureisland
The supervisor stood up straight and stiff and told his story like a | treasureisland
lesson; and you should have seen how the two gentlemen leaned forward | treasureisland
and looked at each other, and forgot to smoke in their surprise and | treasureisland
interest. When they heard how my mother went back to the inn, Dr. | treasureisland
Livesey fairly slapped his thigh, and the squire cried “Bravo!” and | treasureisland
broke his long pipe against the grate. Long before it was done, Mr. | treasureisland
Trelawney (that, you will remember, was the squire's name) had got up | treasureisland
from his seat and was striding about the room, and the doctor, as if to | treasureisland
hear the better, had taken off his powdered wig and sat there looking | treasureisland
very strange indeed with his own close-cropped black poll. | treasureisland
At last Mr. Dance finished the story. | treasureisland
“Mr. Dance,” said the squire, “you are a very noble fellow. And as for | treasureisland
riding down that black, atrocious miscreant, I regard it as an act of | treasureisland
virtue, sir, like stamping on a cockroach. This lad Hawkins is a trump, | treasureisland
I perceive. Hawkins, will you ring that bell? Mr. Dance must have some | treasureisland
ale.” | treasureisland
“And so, Jim,” said the doctor, “you have the thing that they were | treasureisland
after, have you?” | treasureisland
“Here it is, sir,” said I, and gave him the oilskin packet. | treasureisland
The doctor looked it all over, as if his fingers were itching to open | treasureisland
it; but instead of doing that, he put it quietly in the pocket of his | treasureisland
coat. | treasureisland
